 C. Lahoti, J. This batch of matters, some appeals by special leave under Article 136 of the Constitution and some writ petitions filed in this Court, raise a few questions of constitutional significance centering around Entries 52, 54 and 97 in List I and Entries 23, 49, 50 and 66 in List II of the Seventh Schedule to the Constitution of India as also the extent and purport of the residuary power of legislation vested in the Union of India.	Facts
Cesses on coal bearing land, levied in exercise of the power conferred by State Legislation, have been struck down by a Division Bench of the Calcutta High Court.	Facts
In exercise of the same power conferred by State Legislation whereunder cesses were levied on coal bearing land, cesses have also been levied on tea plantation land which are the subjectmatter of writ petitions filed in this Court.	Facts
The Bengal Brickfield Owners Association have also come up to this Court by filing a writ petition under Article 32 of the Constitution, laying challenge to the same cesses levied on the removal of brick earth.	Facts
These three sets of matters arise from West Bengal.	Facts
The High Court of Allahabad has upheld the constitutional validity of cess levied in the State of U.P. on minor minerals which decisions are the subjectmatter of civil appeals filed under Article 136 of the Constitution.	Facts
For the sake of convenience, we would call these matters, respectively as (A) Coal Matters, (B) Tea Matters, (C) Brick Earth Matters and (D) Minor Mineral Matters.	Facts
Inasmuch as the basic constitutional questions arising for decision in all these matters are the same, all the matters have been heard analogously.2.	Facts
We would first set out the facts in brief and so far as relevant for appreciating the issues arising for decision and thereafter deal with the same.(A) Coal Matters3.	Facts
A Division Bench of the Calcutta High Court has, vide its judgment dated 25.11.92 reported as Kesoram Industries Ltd. (Textiles Division) vs. Coal India Ltd., AIR 1993 Calcutta 78, struck down certain levies by way of cess on coal as unconstitutional for want of legislative competence in the State Legislature.	Facts
Feeling aggrieved, the State of West Bengal has come up in appeal by special leave.4.	Facts
The levies which are the subject matter of challenge are as underThe Cess Act, 1980S.5.	Facts
All immovable property to be liable to a road cess and public works cess From and after the commencement of this Act in any district or part of a district, all immovable property situate therein except as otherwise in (Section 2) provided, shall be liable to the payment of a road cess and a public works cess.	Facts
S.6 Cesses how to be assessed.	Facts
The road cess and the public works cess(shall be assessed (a) in respect of lands on the annual value thereof,(b) in respect of all mines and quarries, on the annual dispatches therefrom, and,(c) in respect of tramways, railways and other immovable property, on the annual net profit thereof, ascertained respectively as in this Act prescribed)and the rates at which such cesses respectively shall be levied for each year shall be determined for such year in the manner in this Act prescribedProvided that (1) the rates of such road cess and public works cess shall not excess six paise and twentyfive paise respectively on each rupee of such annual value,(2) the rates of each of such road cess and public works cess shall not exceed (i) fifty paise on each tonne of coal, minerals or sand of such annual dispatches, and(ii) six paise on each rupee of such annual net profits,Explanation.	Facts
For the purposes of this proviso, one tonne of coke shall be counted as one and a quarter tonne of coal.2.	Facts

All the three legislations abovereferred to are State enactments.	Facts

BHATTACHARYYA,Secy. of the Govt, of West Bengal.6.	Facts
It is the constitutional validity of the amendment in the two legislations, given affect to from 1.4.92, which was successfully impugned in the High Court and is sought to be restored in these appeals.7.	Facts
The High Court has placed reliance mainly on two decisions of this Court, namely, India Cement Ltd. and others vs. State of Tamil Nadu and others (1990) 1 SCC 12 (SevenJudges Bench decision) and Orissa Cement Ltd. vs. State of Orissa and others, 1991 (supp.)	Facts
(1) SCC 430 (ThreeJudges Bench decision).	Facts
In both these decisions the levy of cess impugned therein was struck down as unconstitutional.	Facts
The High Court of Calcutta has held that the impugned herein is similar to the one held ultra vires the legislative competence of the State twice by the Supreme Court, and hence the same was liable to be struck down.8.	Facts
In the opinion of the High Court, the cess is assessed and computed on the basis of value of coal produced from the coal bearing land, and coal bearing land has been defined to mean land having one or more seams of coal comprising the area of a coal mine.	Facts
Therefore, it is the production of coal from a coal from a coal mine which is the basic event for the levies and the cess is to be levied at 35 per centum of the annual value of the coal bearing land, which, as per definition, is directly related to the value of the coal produced from the coal mines.	Facts
The value of the coal has been related to the price.	Facts
Explanation to Clause (1) of subSection (2) of the 1922, Act as amended by the 1976 Act, makes the real nature of the levy clearer by providing that where different prices are prevailing on the relevant date of different grades or qualities of coal, the value of the each grade or quality shall be relevant.	Facts
The High Court has concluded that the cess cannot be said to be on land so as to be covered by Entry 49 in Schedule II.	Facts
On behalf of the writ petitioner  respondents, the judgment of the High Court has been supported on similar grounds as were successfully urged before the High Court and which we shall presently deal with.	Facts
On the other hand, the learned counsel for the appellantState of West Bengal has submitted that having regard to the real nature of the levy, it clearly falls within the legislative field of Entry 49 in List II.(B)	Facts
Tea matters9.	Facts
The writ petitions in such the validity of the levy of cesses relatable to tea estates is involved has an interesting legislative history behind it.	Facts
By virtue of the West Bengal Taxation Laws (Amendment) Act, 1981, amendments were affected in the provisions of the West Bengal Primary Education Act, 1973, and the West Bengal Rural employment and Production Act, 1976.	Facts
Cesses were sought to be levied upon certain lands and buildings in the State for raising funds for the purpose of providing primary education throughout the State and to provide for employment in rural areas.	Facts
Different rates in respect of lands, coal mines and other mines on annual basis were provided.	Facts
tea estates were carved out as a separate category and a separate rate was prescribed therefor as under.10.	Facts
Section 4(2)	Facts
The rural employment cess shall be levied annually (a) in respect of lands, other than a tea estate, at the rate of six paise on each rupee of development value thereof(aa) in respect of a tea estate at such rate not exceeding ruppes six on each kilogram of tea on the despatches, from such tea estate of tea grown therein, as the State Government may, by notification in the Official Gazette, fix in this behalfProvided that in calculating the despatches of tea for the purpose of levy of rural employment cess, such despatches for sale made at such tea auction centers as may be recognised by the State Government by notification in the Official Gazette shall be excludedProvided further that the State Government, may fix different rates on despatches of different classes of tea.	Facts
Explanation  For the purpose of this section, tea means the plant Camelia Sinensis (L) O. Kuntza as well as all varieties of the product known commercially as tea made from the leaves of the plant Camelia Sinensis (L) O. Kuntze, including green tea and green tea leaves, processed or unprocessed.	Facts
Subsection (4) was introduced in Section 4 which empowered the State Government to exempt such categories of dispatches or such percentage of dispatches from the liability to pay the whole or any part of the rural employment cess or reduce the rate By another amendment effected in 1982, the first proviso to clause (aa) in Section 4(2) was omitted.	Facts
Several notifications were issued by the Government from time to time as contemplated by Section 4(2).11.	Facts
The constitutional validity of the abovesaid amendment was challenged successfully in Buxa Dooars Tea Company Ltd. and others vs. State of West Bengal and others (1989) 3 SCC 211.	Facts
The decision is by a Bench of two learned Judges.	Facts
The levy of cess having struck down, the State became liable to refund the cess already collected and the relevant schemes which were financed by the cessess so collected came under jeopardy.	Facts
The West Bengal Taxation Laws (Second Amendment) Act, 1989 was enacted, which is under challenge herein.12.	Facts
Section 2 of the impugned Act contains amendments to West Bengal Primary Education Act while Section 3 sets out the amendments to West Bengal Rural Employment and Production Act, 1976.	Facts
As amendment hereinafter, it would be enough to notice the gist of the amendments made in one of the two Acts of 1976 since the amendments in both are identical.13.	Facts
Clause (aa) in subsection (2) of Section 4 was omitted with effect from 1.4.1981.	Facts
After subsection (2), subsection (2A) was introduced with retrospective effect from 1.4.1981.	Facts
SubSection (2A) reads(2A)	Facts
The rural employment cess shall be levied annually on a tea estate at the rate of twelve paise for each kilogram of green tea leaves produced in such estate.	Facts
Explanation  For the purposes of this subsection, subsection (3) and Section 4B (i) green tea leaves shall mean the plucked and unprocessed green leaves of the plant Camelia Sinensis (L) O. Kuntze(ii) tea estate shall mean any land used or intended to be used for growing plant Camelia Sinensis (L) O. Kuntze and producing green tea leaves from such plant, and shall include land comprised in a factory or workshop for producing any variety of the product known commercially as tea made from the leaves of such plant and for housing the persons employed in the tea estate and other lands for purposes ancillary to the growing of such plant and producing green tea leaves from such plant.14.	Facts
Clause (a) in subsection (3) was also substituted which had the effect of making the owner of the tea estate liable for the said cess.	Facts
The other provisions require the owner of the tea estate to maintain a true and correct account of green tea leaves produced in the tea estate.	Facts
Subsection (4) was also substituted.	Facts
The substituted subsection (4) empowered the State Government to exempt from the cess such categories of tea estates producing green tea leaves not exceeding two lakh fifty thousand kilograms and located in such area as may be specified in such notification.	Facts
Section 4B contains the validation clause.	Facts
It says that any cess collected for the period prior to the said Amendment Act shall be deemed to have been validly levied by it and collected under the Amended Act.	Facts
Any assessment made or other proceedings taken in that behalf for assessing and collecting the said tax were also to be deemed to have been taken under the Amended Act.15.	Facts
Goodricke Group Ltd. and other filed a writ petition under Article 32 of the Constitution of India in this Court.	Facts
The levy of cesses under both the State enactments as amended by the West Bengal Taxation Laws (SecondAmendment) Act, 1989 was impugned.	Facts
A few matters raising a similar challenge and pending in various High Courts were also withdrawn to this Court.	Facts
All the matters were heard and decided by a threeJudges Bench of this Court, vide judgment dated November 25, 1994, reported in Goodricke Group Ltd. and others vs. State of West Bengal and others (1995)	Facts
Supp.	Facts
1 SCC 707.	Facts
The decision of this Court in India Cement Ltd. and others vs. State of Tamil Nadu and others (1990) 1 SCC 12 (sevenJudges Bench) and Orissa Cement Limited vs. State of Orissa and others (1991) Suppl.1 SCC 430 (threeJudges Bench) were cited before the threejudges Bench in Goodricke.	Facts
Both the decisions were distinguished and the constitutional validity of the 1989 amendments was upheld.	Facts
The writ petitions were dismissed.16.	Facts
It appears that a similar cess was levied by a pari materia provision enacted by the State Legislature of Orissa as the Orissa Rural Employment, Education and Production Act, 1982.	Facts
The cess was on land being coal and minerals.	Facts
Challenge to the constitutional validity of such cess was successfully laid before this Court, and the Orissa Legislation was struck down as unconstitutional and ultra vires the compentence of the State Legislature in State of Orissa vs. Mahanadi Coal Fields Limited (1995) Suppl.	Facts
2 SCC 686 decided on April 21, 1995.17.	Facts
On 30.3.1996 a writ petition under Article 32 of the Constitution of India has been filed in this Court laying challenge to the constitutional validity of the very same amendments which were unsuccessfully impugned in the Goodrickes case.18.	Facts
The writ petitions in the Tea Matters have in their petition stated a few grounds in support of the relief sought for.	Facts
However, a perusal of the grounds reveals that in substance the challenges is only one, i.e. the decision in Goodricke runs counter to the view of the law taken by SevenJudges Bench in India Cement and threeJudges Bench in Orissa Cement Goodricke was rightly not followed in Mahanadi Coal Fields rather Mahanadi Coal Fields has whittled down the authority of Goodricke and that being the position of law the impugned cess is ultra vires the power of the State Legislature and deserves to be pronounced so.	Facts
In short, the same challenge as was laid and turned down in Goodricke, is reiterated drawing support from the decisions of this Court previous and subsequent to Goodricke, and seeks the overruing of Goodricke.(C) BrickEarth Matters19.	Facts
The Bengal Brickfield Owners Association, being a representative body of the persons engaged in the activity of brick manufacturing and owning brickfields as also one of the brickfield owners, have joined in filing a writ petition before this Court wherein the constitutional validity of the very same provisions as contained in the Cess Act, 1880, the West Bengal Primary Education Act, 1973 and the West Bengal Rural Employment and Production Act, 1976 (both as amended by the Bengal Taxation Laws Amendment Act, 1992) has been put in issue, as has been subjected to challenge by the coal mine owners and the tea estate owners disputing the levy of cess allegedly on coal and tea.	Facts
The grounds of challenge, briefly stated, are three on number firstly, that brickearth is a minor mineral to which the Mines and Minerals Development and Regulation Act, 1957, applies and by virtue of the declaration made by Section 2 of the Act by reference to Entry 54 in Schedule I of the Constitution, the field relating to such minor minerals is entirely covered by the Central Legislation and hence the State Legislations are not competent to levy the impugned cess secondly, that the levy is on the dispatch of minor minerals for sale while the process of manufacturing bricks does not involve any dispatch of the brickearth inasmcuh as the brickearth is consumed then and there, on the brickfield itself, in the process of manufacturing of bricks, and there being no dispatch or brickearth, the cess is not leviable and thirdly, that the State Government is not empowered to levy any cess on either the extraction of brickearth or on the dispatch of brickearth.	Facts
In support of these three grounds, it is further submitted that the same quantity of brickearth is subjected by Central Legislation to payment of royalty which is a tax, and the same quantity of brickearth is sought to be levied with cess which is incompetent so far as the State Government is concerned.	Facts
The writ petition places reliance on the decisions of this Court in India Cement Ltd. and others (supra), Orissa Cement Ltd. (supra) and Buxa Dooars Tea Company Ltd. and other (supra).	Facts
Some of the members of the petitioner association were served with demand notices.	Facts
The relief sought for in the petition is striking down of the relevant provisions of the three State Legislations as ultra vires the Constitution and quashing of the demand notices.	Facts
The reason for filing the petition in this Court, as stated in the writ petition, is that the provisions sought to be impugned herein have already been declared ultra vires by the High Court of Calcutta in relation to tea, an appeal against which decision has been filed in this Court and by an interim order the operation of the Judgment of the High Court was stayed.20.	Facts
According to the respondents, the cess sought to be levied by the impugned State Legislation is in the nature of fee and not tax.	Facts
The purpose of levying fee, as stated in the Preamble to the relevant legislation, is rendering different services to the society and for public benefit.	Facts
The cesses have been levied by the State Government for securing of welfare to the people by the State as is enshrined in Part IV of the Constitution of India by providing communication facilities, removal of illiteracy and rural employment to the poor living below the poverty line.	Facts
The impugned legislations levying the cess, do not encroach upon the field covered by the Central legislation.	Facts
The brickklin owners extract the brickearth as an item of trade.	Facts
From every 100 cft of brickearth which weighs 5 metric tones, 1382 bricks are manufactured.	Facts
The dispatch of 1382 bricks means the dispatch of 100 cft or 5 metric tones of brickearth.	Facts
A brickfield owner performs dual functions firstly, he extracts a quantum of brickearth from the quarry, and secondly, he dispatches the same for manufacture of bricks in the same quarryfield.	Facts
The brickfield owner is an extractor of brickearth and also a manufacturer of bricks.	Facts
The element of dispatch is kept hidden.	Facts
That is why the cess is now assessed on annual dispatches.	Facts
Dispatch, in the context of brickearth, means a removal of brickearth from one place to another which may be within the same complex and for domestic or captive use or consumption.	Facts
In any case, the removal of brickearth involved in the process cannot escape assessment.	None
Minor Mineral Matters This batch of appeals puts in issue the judgment dated 1.3.2000 delivered by a Division Bench of the Allahabad High Court (reported as Ram Dhani Singh vs. Collector, Sonbhadra and others (AIR 2001 Allahabad 5), upholding the constitutional validity of a cess on mineral rights levied under Section 35 of the U.P. Special Area Development Authorities Act, 1986, read with Rule 3 of the Shakti Nagar Special Area Development Authority (Cess on Mineral Rights) Rules, 1997 (herein referred to briefly as SADA Act and SADA Cess Rules respectively).	Facts
There was a bunch of 73 writ petitions filed in the High Court which have all been dismissed.	Facts
The challenge is being pursued in this Court by ten writ petitioners through these appeals by special leave.22.	Facts
The Governor of Uttar Pradesh promulgated U.P. Ordinance No. 15 of 1985, which was repealed by U.P. Special Area Development Authorities Act, 1986 (U.P. Act No. 9 of 1986), containing identical provisions as were contained in the preceding Ordinance.	Facts

Relevant Entries and principles of interpretation Before we proceed to examine the merits of the submissions and counter submissions made on behalf the parties, it will be useful to recapitulate and summarize a few principles relevant for interpreting entries classified and grouped into the three Lists of the Seventh Schedule of the Constitution.	None
The law is legion on the point and the principles which are being briefly stated hereinafter are more than settled.	None
These principles are referred to in the several decisions which we shall be referring to hereinafter.	None
So far as the principles are concerned, they have been followed invariably in all the decisions, however, diverse results have followed based on facts of individual cases and manner of application of such principles to the facts of those cases.	None
The relevant entries to which reference would be required to be made during the course of this judgment are extracted and reproduced herein SEVENTH SCHEDULE (Article 246)	None
List I Union List Industries, the control of which by the Union is declared by Parliament by law to be expedient in the public interest.	None
Regulation of mines and mineral development to the extent to which such regulation and development under the control of the Union is declared by Parliament by law to be expedient in the public interest.	None
Fees is respect of any of the matters in this List, but not including fees taken in any court.	None
Any other matter not enumerated in List II or List III including any tax not mentioned in either of those lists.	None
List II State List Regulation of mines and mineral development subject to the provisions of List I with respect to regulation and development under the control of the Union.	Reasoning
Taxes on lands and buildings.	None
Taxes on lands and buildings.	None
Taxes on mineral rights subject to any limitations imposed by Parliament by law relating to mineral development.	None
Fees in respect of any of the matter in this List, but not including fees taken in any court.	None
Article 245 of the Constitution is the fountain source of legislative power.	Reasoning
It provides  subject to the provisions of this Constitution, Parliament may make laws for the whole or any part of the territory of India, and the Legislature of a State may make laws for the whole or any part of the State.	Reasoning
The legislative field between the Parliament and the Legislature of any State is divided by Article 246 of the Constitution.	Reasoning
Parliament has exclusive power to make laws with respect to any of the matters enumerated in List I in Seventh Schedule, called the Union List.	Reasoning
Subject to the said power of the Parliament, the Legislature of any State has power to make laws with respect to any of the matters enumerated in List III, called the Concurrent List.	Reasoning
Subject to the above said two, the Legislature of any State has exclusive power to make laws with respect to any of the matters enumerated in List II, called the State List.	None
Under Article 248 the exclusive power to Parliament to make laws extends to any matter not enumerated in the Government List or State List.	Reasoning
The power to making any law imposing of tax not mentioned in the Concurrent List or State List vests in Parliament.	Reasoning
This is what is called the residuary power vesting in Parliament.	Reasoning
The principles have been succinctly summarized and restated by a Bench of three learned Judges of this Court on a review of the available decisions in M s. Hoechst Pharmaceuticals Ltd. and others vs. State of Bihar and others  (1983) 4 SCC 45.	Reasoning
They are (1) the various entries in the three Lists are not powers of legislation but fields of legislation.	Reasoning
The Constitution effects a complete separation of the taxing power of the Union and of the States under Article 246.	Reasoning
There is no overlapping anywhere in the taxing power and the Constitution gives independent sources of taxation to the Union and the States.(2)	Reasoning
In spite of the fields of legislation having been demarcated, the question of repugnancy between law made by Parliament and a law made by the State Legislature may arise only in cases when both the legislations occupy the same field with respect to one of the matters enumerated in the Concurrent List and a direct conflict is seen.	Reasoning
If there is a repugnancy due to overlapping found between List II on the one hand and List I and List III on the other, the State law will be ultra vires and shall have to give way to the Union law.(3) Taxation is considered to be a distinct matter for purposes of legislative competence.	Reasoning
There is a distinction made between general subjects of legislation and taxation.	Reasoning
The general subjects of legislation are dealt with in one group of entries and power of taxation in a separate group.	Reasoning
The power to tax cannot be deduced from a general legislative entry as an ancillary power.(4)	Reasoning
The entries in the List being merely topics of fields of legislation, they must receive a liberal construction inspired by a board and generous spirit and not in a narrow pedantic sense.	Reasoning
The words and expressions employed in drafting the entries must be given the widest possible interpretation.	Reasoning
This is because, to quote V. Ramaswami, J., the allocation of the subjects to the lists is not by way of scientific or logical definition but by way of a mere simplex enumeration of broad categories.	Reasoning
A power to legislate as to the principle matter specifically mentioned in the entry shall also include within its expense the legislations touching incidental and ancillary matters.(5) Where the legislative competence of a Legislature of any State is questioned on the ground that it encroaches upon the legislative competence of Parliament to enact a law, the question one has to ask is whether the legislation relates to any of the entries in Lists I or III.	Reasoning
If it does, no further question need be asked the Parliaments legislative competence must be upheld.	None
Where there are three Lists containing a large number of entries, there is bound to be some overlapping among them.	Reasoning
In such a situation the doctrine of pith and substance has to be applied to determine as to which entry does a given piece of legislation relate.	Reasoning
Once it is so determined, any incidental trenching on the field reserved to the other Legislature is of no consequence.	Reasoning
The Court has to look at the substance of the matter.	Reasoning
The doctrine of pith and substance is sometimes expressed in terms of ascertaining the true character of legislation.	Reasoning
The name given by the Legislature to the legislation is immaterial.	Reasoning
Regard must be had to the enactment as a whole, to its main objects and to the scope and effect of its provisions.	Reasoning
Incidental and superficial encroachments are to be disregarded.(6)	Reasoning
The doctrine of occupied field applies only when there is a clash between the Union and the State Lists within an area common to both.	Reasoning
There the doctrine of pith and substance is to be applied and if the impugned legislation substantially falls within the power expressly conferred upon the Legislature which enacted it, an incidental encroaching in the field assigned to another Legislature is to be ignored.	Reasoning
While reading the three Lists, List I has priority over lists III and II, and List III has priority over List II.	Reasoning
However, still, the predominance of the Union List would not prevent the State Legislature from dealing with any matter within List II though it may incidentally affect any item in List I. (emphasis supplied)	None
Tax Legislation	None
The above stated are general principles.	None
Legislations in the field of taxation and economic activities need special consideration and are to be viewed with larger flexibility in approach.	None
Observations of the Constitution Bench in R.K. Garg vs. Union of India and others, (1981) 4 SCC 676, are apposite, wherein this Court has emphasized a greater latitude  like play in the joints  being allowed to the Legislature because it has to be deal with complex problems which do not admit of solution through any doctrinaire or straitjacket formula.	None
In this field the Court should feel more inclined to give judicial defence to legislative judgment.	None
Their Lordships quoted with approval the following statement of Frankfurter, J. in Morey vs. Daud, (1957) 354 US 457 In the utilities, tax and economic regulation cases, there are good reasons for judicial selfrestraint if not judicial deference to legislative judgment.	None
The legislature after all has the affirmative responsibility.	None
The Courts have only the power to destroy not to reconstruct.	None
When these are added to the complexity or economic regulation, the uncertainty, the liability to error the bewildering conflict of the experts, and the number of times the judges have been overruled by events, selflimitation can be seen to be the path to judicial wisdom and institutional prestige and stability.	None
Their Lordships further observed that the Courts ought to adopt a pragmatic approach in solving problems rather than measuring the propositions by abstract symmetry.	None
The exact wisdom and nice adaptations of remedies may not be possible.	None
Even crudities and inequities have to be accommodated in complicated tax and economic legislations.	None
We now proceed to enter a deeper dimension in the field of tax legislation by considering the problem of devising the measure of taxation.	None
This aspect has been dealt with in dealt in Union of India and others vs. Bombay Tyre International Ltd., (1983) 4 SCC 210.	Reasoning
Tracing the principles from the leading authority of Re. a reference under the Government of Ireland Act 1920 and Section 3 of the Finance Act (Northern Ireland) 1934, (1936)	Reasoning
A.C. 352, passing through Relia Ram vs. Province of East Punjab, 1948 FCR 207, and treading through the law as it has developed through judicial pronouncements one after the other, this Court has made subtle observations therein.	Reasoning
It has been long recognized that the measure employed for assessing a tax must not be confused with the nature of the tax.	Reasoning
A tax has two elements first, the person, thing or activity on which the tax is imposed, and secondly, the amount of tax.	Reasoning
The amount may be measured in many ways but a distinction between the subject matter of a tax and the standard by which the amount of tax is measured must not be lost sight of.	Reasoning
These are described respectively as the subject of a tax and the measure of a tax.	Reasoning
It is true that the standard adopted as a measure of the levy may be indicative of the nature of the tax, but it does not necessary determine it.	Reasoning
The nature of the mechanism by which the tax is to be assessed is not decisive of the essential characteristic of the particular tax charged, thought it may throw light on the general character of the tax.	Reasoning
Here we may refer to certain illustrative cases of well settled authority  the authority which has not been shaken so far and has rather withstood the test of times.	None
Taxation  Measure of levy not suggestive of nature of tax  illustrative cases  In Ralla Ram (supra) the Federal Court held that a tax on buildings under Section 3 of the Punjab Urban Immovable Property Tax Act, 1940, measured by a percentage of the annual value of such building, remained a tax on buildings even though the measure of annual value of a building was also adopted as a standard for determining income from property under the Income Tax Act.	None
The same standard was adopted as a measure for the two levies, yet the levies remained separate imposts by virtue of their distinctive nature.	None
The measure adopted, it was held, could not be identified with the nature of the tax levied.	None
In M s. Sainik Motors, Jodhpur vs. State of Rajasthan (1962) 1	None
SCR 517 , a tax on passengers and goods was assessed as a rate on the fares and freights payable by the owners of the motor vehicles.	None
The contention that the levy was a tax upon income and not upon passengers and goods was repelled by this Court.	None
The Court pointed out that though the measure of the tax is furnished by the fares and freights it does not cease to be a tax on passengers and goods.	None
In D.G. Gouse Co., State of Kerala, (1980) 2 SCC 410 , the Court examined the different modes available to the Legislature for measuring the levy of tax on buildings.	None
The Court upheld the provision made by the Legislature linking the levy with the annual value of the building and prescribing a uniformed formula for determining its capital value and for calculating the tax.	None
In The HingirRampur Coal Co. Ltd. vs. State of Orissa, 1961) 2	Reasoning
SCR 537 , the form in which the levy was imposed was held to be an impermissible test for defining in itself the character of the levy.	None
It was argued that the method of determining the rate of levy was by reference to the minerals produced by the mines and, therefore, it was levy in the nature of a duty of excise.	None
This Court held that the method thus adopted may be relevant in considering the character of the impost but its effect must be weighed along with and in the light of the other relevant circumstances.	None
Referring to Bombay Tyre International Ltd. (supra), the Court further held that it is clear that when enacting a measure to serve as a standard for assessing the levy, the Legislature need not contour it along lines which spell out the character of the levy itself.	None
A broader based standard of reference is permissible to be adopted for the purpose of determining the measure of the levy.	None
Any standard which maintains a nexus with the essential character of the levy can be regarded as a valid basis for assessing the measure of the levy.	None
Meaning of Lands  as used in Entry 49 in List II	None
The word land  as used in Entry 49 in List II, came up for the consideration of this Court in Anant Mills vs. State of Gujarat, (1995) 2 SCC 175.	None
It was held that the word land cannot be assigned a narrow meaning so as to confine it to the surface of the earth.	None
It includes all strata above or below.	None
In other words, the word land includes not only the surface of the earth but everything under or over it, and has in its legal significance an indefinite extent upward and downward.	None
The fourJudges Bench upheld the validity of the law levying tax in respect of area occupied by underground lines by reference to Entry 49 in List II, holding it to be a tax on land only.	None
Ample authority is available for the concept that under Entry 49 in List II the land remains a land without regard to the use to which it is being subjected.	None
It is open for the Legislature to ignore the nature of the user and tax the land.	None
At the same time it is also permissible to identify, for the purpose of classification, the land by reference to its user.	None
While taxing the land it is open for the Legislature to consider the land which produces a particular growth or is useful for a particular utility and to classify it separately and tax the same.	Reasoning
Different pieces of land identically situated otherwise, but being subjected to different uses, or having different potential, are capable of being classified separately without incurring the wrath of Article 14 of the Constitution.	None
The Constitution Bench in Kunnathat Thathunnai Moonil Nair etc. vs. State of Kerala and Another (1961) 3	None
SCR 77 , held that the land on which a forest stands is not to be excluded necessarily from Entry 409.	None
The erstwhile Entry 19 of Schedule II applied to forest.	None
Their Lordships held that the use of the word forest in Entry 19 could not be pressed into service to cut down the plain meaning of the word land in Entry 49.	None
It was permissible to tax the land on which a forest stands by reference to Entry 40.	None
In Ajoy Kumar Mukherjee vs. Local Board of Barpeta (1965) 3 SCR 47 , the appellant, a land holder, held a hatt (or market) on his land.	None
The Local Board asked the appellant to take out a licence and pay Rs. 600/, later Rs. 700/ by way of licence fee for holding the market.	None
It was urged that the impost was unconstitutional, inter alia, on the ground that the tax was actually imposed on the market, which infringed Article 14 of the Constitution and also because the State Legislature had no legislative competence to tax a market.	None
The Local Board relied on Entry 49 in List II.	None
The appellant urged that Entries 45 to 63 which deal with taxes do not contemplate a tax on markets.	None
Repelling the plea, the Constitution Bench held that the tax was on the land though the charges arise only when the land is used for a market.	None
The tax remained a tax on land in spite of the imposition being dependant upon the user of the land as a market.	None
The tax was an annual tax as contrasted to a tax for each day on which the market was held.	None
The owner or occupier of the land was responsible for payment of tax on a annual basis.	None
The amount of tax depended upon the area of the land on which the market was held and the importance of the market.	None
Thus, the tax was held to be a tax on land, though the incidence depended upon the use of the land as a market.	None
In Vivian Joseph Ferreira and Anr.	None
vs. The Municipal Corporation of Greater Bombay and others (1972) 1 SCC 70 , the tax was confined to the residential tenanted buildings.	None
The classification was held to be valid.	None
In the Government of Andhra Pradesh and another vs. Hindustan Machine Tools Ltd. (1975) 2 SCC 274 houses tax was levied on the buildings.	None
The new definition of house included a factory.	None
However, the house tax was levied only on the building occupied by the factory and not on the machinery and furniture.	None
The State Legislature claimed competence to do so under Entry 49, List II.	None
The power to tax a building, exercisable without reference to the use to which the building is put, was held to be valid.	None
In the opinion of the Court, it was irrelevant that the building was occupied by a factory which could not conduct its activities without the machinery and furniture.	None
Once it is held that the land or building is available to be taxed, it does not matter to what use the land is being subjected though the nature of the user may enable land of one particular user being classified separately from the land being subjected to another kind of user.	None
The tax would remain a tax on land.	None
It cannot be urged that what is being taxed is not the land but the nature of its user.	None
So also it is permissible to adopt myriad forms and methods of valuation for the purpose of quantifying the tax.	None
In Ralla Ram vs. The Province of East Punjabu  1948 FCR 207, the Federal Court made it clear that every effort should be made as far as possible to reconcile the seeming conflict between the provisions of the Provincial Legislation and the Federal Legislation.	None
Unless the court forms an opinion that the extent of the alleged invasion by a Provincial Legislature into the field of the Federal Legislature is so great as would justify the view that in pith and substance of the impugned tax is a tax within the domain of the Federal Legislature, the levy of tax would not be liable to be struck down.	None
The test laid down in Sir Byramjee Jeejeebhoys case (AIR 1940 Bom 65 ) by the Full Bench of Bombay High Court was approved.	None
In Assistant Commissioner of Urban Land Tax Madras and others etc. vs. Buckingham and Carnatic Co. Ltd. etc.  (1969) 2 SCC 55 , for the purpose of attracting the applicability of Entry 49 in List II, so as to cover the impugned levy of tax on lands and buildings, the Constitution Bench laid down twin tests, namely, (i) that such tax is directly imposed on lands and buildings, and (ii) that it bears a definite relation to it.	None
Once these tests were satisfied, it was open for the State Legislature, for the purpose of levying tax, to adopt the annual value or the capital value of the lands and buildings for determining the incidence of tax.	None
Merely, on account of such methodology having been adopted, the State Legislature cannot be accused of having encroached upon Entries 86, 87 or 88 of List I. Entry 86 in List I proceeds on the Principle of Aggregation and tax is imposed on the totality of the value of all the assets.	None
It is quite permissible to separate lands and buildings for the purpose of taxation under Entry 49 in List II.	None
There is no reason for restricting the amplitude of the language used in the Entry 49 in List II.	None
The levy of tax, calculated at the rate of a certain per centum of the market value of the urban land was held to be intra vires the powers of the State Legislature and not trenching upon Entry 86 in List I.	None
So is the view taken by another Constitution Bench of Shri Prithvi Cotton Mills Ltd. , etc. vs. Breach Borough Municipality and others, (1969) 2 SCC 283 , where the submission that the levy was not a rate on lands and buildings as appropriately understood, but rather a tax on capital value was discarded.	None
M s. R.R. Engineering Co. etc.	None
Zila Parishad, Bareilly and Anr.	None
etc.  (1980) 3 SCC 330 , is a case of circumstances and properties tax levied on the basis of income which the assessee receives from his profession, trade, calling or property.	None
The plea that the tax was a tax on income was discarded.	None
The test propounded by the Constitution Bench is that an excessive levy on circumstance may tend to blur the distinction between a tax on income and a tax on circumstances.	None
Income will then cease to be a measure or yardstick of the tax and will become the very subjectmatter of the tax.	None
Restraint in this behalf is a prudent prescription for the local authorities to follow.	None
The Constitution Bench observed that it was only a matter of convenience that income was adopted as a yardstick or measure for assessing the tax and the evolvement of such mechanism was not conclusive on the nature of tax.	None
We are inclined to make a reference to a few selected Full Bench decisions of different High Courts which have been cited with approval before this Court in many of the decisions to which we are making reference during the course of this judgment.	None
In Sir Byramjee Jeejeebhoy vs. Province of Bombay and Ors.  A.I.R. 1940 Bombay 65 (F.B.) the Provincial Government levied a tax at the rate of 5 of the annual letting value of the City of Bombay on the buildings and lands.	None
The buildings were classified by reference to their annual letting value, and exception from payment of tax was also carved out in favour of such buildings as remained vacant and unproductive of rent for the specified period.	None
It was urged that the impugned tax purported or desired to tax the value.	None
Placing reliance on the Federal Courts decision in In Re C.P. Motor Spirit Act, 1939 (1939 FCR 18) Chief Justice Beaumont held that the impugned tax was a tax on lands and buildings.	None
Three submissions were made in support of the challenge (i) that the tax is graded by reference to the annual value of the property charged, (ii) that an allowance was available to be made in respect of vacant properties, and (iii) that the basis of the tax was the same as the basis on which tax on income from property was imposed by Sections 6 and 9 of Income Tax Act and, therefore in reality the rate was a tax on income.	None
Beaumont, C.J. held that regard must be had to the pith and substance of the impugned tax and not merely to the form.	None
All the items in the Provincial List must be so construed as to exclude taxes on income.	None
The tax is charged on lands and buildings and it is based on the estimated rent which the property would fetch.	None
Such a value may bear very little relation to the actual income of the property.	None
It is imposed without any relation to the capital value except insofar as such value can be ascertained by reference to the rateable value.	None
It did not make any difference if the arbitrary basis which was adopted for the purpose of the rate might as well be applied for ascertaining the capital value as for ascertaining income.	None
The fact that some concession is allowed to the small owner, a concession which may be based as much on political as on economic considerations and that an allowance may be made where the property is shown to produce no income, a fact which may be taken to show that the estimated value was found to be erroneous, cannot alter the nature of the tax.	None
The concept that in case of conflict between the Federal List and Provincial List, an entry in the Federal List may be given a more resticted meaning, was endorsed.	None
The legality of the levy was upheld.	None
In District Board of Farrukhabad vs. Prag Dutt and Ors.	None
AIR 1948 Allahabad 382 (F.B.), a tax on circumstances and property was under challenge.	None
It was urged that it was a tax on income.	None
Chief Justice Malik held that the fundamental difference between the tax on income and a tax on circumstances and property is that income tax can only be levied if there is income and if there is no income, no tax is payable.	None
But in the case of circumstances and property tax, where a mans status has to be determined, his total business turnover may be considered for purposes of taxation, though he may not have earned any taxable income.	None
The State of Punjab vs. The Union of India through the Secretary to Government Finance Department, Government of India, New Delhi  AIR 1971 Punjab Haryana 155 (F.B.), is a FiveJudges Bench decision delivered by Chief Justice Harbans Singh.	None
Conflict was noticed between List I, Entry 86 and List II, Entry 49.	None
Dealing with the scope of Entry 49 in List II, it was held that it empowers the State Legislatures to directly tax lands and buildings, and for determining the basis of the tax the State Legislature may take either the area, annual rental value, market value of the capital value of the land as a basis for calculating and quantifying the tax on land.	None
Merely because tax was calculated on the basis of annual rental value, it will not turn it into a tax on income, and if it is based on capital value, it will not turn it into a tax on capital value.	None
Yet another angle which the Constitutional Courts would advisedly do better to keep in view while dealing with a tax legislation, in the light of the purported conflict between the powers of the Union and the State to legislate, which was stated forcefully and which was logically based on an analytical examination of constitutional scheme by Jeevan Reddy, J. in S.R. Bomai and others vs. Union of India (1994) 3 SCC 1 , may be touched.	Reasoning
Our Constitution has a federal structure.	Reasoning
Several provisions of the Constitution unmistakably show that the Founding Fathers intended to create a strong centre.	Reasoning
The historical background relevant at the time of the framing of the Constitution warranted a strong centre naturally and necessarily.	Reasoning
This bias of the framers towards the centre is found reflect in the distribution of legislative heads between the Centre and the States.	Reasoning
More important heads of the legislation are placed in List I.	Reasoning
In the Concurrent List the parliamentary enactment is given primacy, irrespective of the fact whether such enactment is earlier or later in point of time to a State enactment on the same subject matter.	Reasoning
The residuary power to legislate is with the Centre.	Reasoning
By the Fortysecond Amendment a few of the entries in List II were omitted or transferred to other lists.	Reasoning
Articles 249 to 252 further demonstrate the primacy of Parliament, allowing it liberty to encroach on the field meant exclusively for the State legislation though subject to certain conditions being satisfied.	Reasoning
In the matter of finances, the States appear to have been placed in a less favourable position.	Reasoning
True, the Centre has been given more powers but the same is accompanied by certain additional responsibilities as well.	Reasoning
The Constitution is an organic living document.	Reasoning
Its outlook and expression as perceived and expressed by the interpreters of the Constitution must be dynamic and keep pace with the changing times.	Reasoning
Though the basics and fundamentals of the Constitution remain unalterable, the interpretation of the flexible provisions of the Constitution can be accompanied by dynamism and lean, in case of conflict, in favour of the weaker or the one who is more needy.	Reasoning
Several taxes are collected by the Centre and allocation of revenue is made to States from time to time.	Reasoning
The Centre consuming the lions share of revenue has attracted good amount of criticism at the hands of the States and financial experts.	Reasoning
The interpretation of Entries can afford to strike a balance, or at least try to remove imbalance, so far as it can.	Reasoning
Any conscious whittling down of the powers of the State can be guarded against by the Courts.	Reasoning
Let it be said that the federalism in the Indian Constitution is not a matter of administrative convenience, but one of principle  the outcome of our own historical process and a recognition of the ground realities.	Reasoning
Quoting from M.C. Setalvad, Tagore Law Lectures Union and State relations under the Indian Constitution (Eastern Law House, Calcutta, 1974), Jeevan Reddy, J. observed  It is enough to note that our Constitution has certainly a bias towards the Centre visavis the States	Reasoning
It is equally necessary to emphasise that Courts should be careful not to upset the delicately  crafted constitutional scheme by a process of interpretation.	None
The Conflict a cautious evaluation of India Cement We will now refer to and deal with those cases which have led to the three learned Judges of this Court, placing the matter for consideration by a Constitution Bench.	None
We would refer to the cases mentioned in the order of reference and also to those cases which were heavily relied upon on behalf of the respondents, disputing the validity of the impugned tax.	None
Immediately, we take up India Cement.	None
In India Cement Ltd. and others vs. State of Tamil Nadu and others (1990) 1 SCC 12 , what was impugned was a levy of cess on royalty and the question was, whether such cess on royalty is within the competence of the State Legislature.	None
The appellant was required to pay, by the Madras Panchayats Act, 1958, local cess at the rate of 45 paise per rupee of the royalty already being paid.	None
The question formulated by the Court, as arising for decision was is cess (on royalty a demand of land revenue or additional royalty?	None
The Court found that the royalty was payable by the appellant as prescribed under the lease deed.	None
The rates of the royalty were fixed under the Mines and Minerals (Development and Regulation) Act, 1957, which is a Central Act, passed under Entry 54 in List I, by which the control of mines and minerals has been taken over by the Central Government.	None
The State Legislature sought to justify and sustain the levy by reference to Entry 49, 50 or 45 in List II.	None
Cess is a tax and is generally used when the levy is for some special administrative expense, suggested by the name of the cess, such as health cess, education cess, road cess etc.	None
This is a wellsettled position of law.	None
The levy was sought to be justified under Entry 45 in List II by including it within the meaning of land revenue, and in the alternative under Entry 49 in List II as tax on lands.	None
The challenge to the constitutional validity of the levy was upheld.	None
We would briefly state the reasoning which prevailed with the learned Judges.	None
G.L. Oza, J. delivered concurring opinion.	None
The majority opinion expressed through Sabyasachi Mukharji, J., (as his Lordship then was), first clarified the distinction between royalty and land revenue, Land revenue is connotative of the share in the produce of land which the king or the Government is entitled to receive.	None
Royalty is a charge payable on the extraction of minerals from the land.	None
A cess on royalty cannot, therefore, be called additional land revenue and as such the State was disabled from imposing tax on royalty.	None
There is a clear distinction between tax directly on land and tax on income arising from land.	None
Royalty is indirectly connected with land and a cess on royalty cannot be called a tax directly on land as a unit.	None
The levy could also not be sustained under Entry 50 in List II which deals with taxes on mineral rights subject to limitation imposed by Parliament relating to mineral development.	None
Assuming that the tax in pith and substance fell to Entry 50 in List II, it would be controlled by a Legislation under Entry 54 in List I. A Division Bench decision of Mysore High Court in M s. Laxminarayana Mining Co., Bangalore and Anr.	None
vs. Taluk Development Board and Anr  AIR 1972	None
Mysore 299 was cited with approval in India Cement.	None
The Mysore High Court struck down as violative of MMDR Act, 1957 a licence fee on mining manganese or iron ore etc. imposed by a State Legislation.	None
A perusal of judgment of the Mysore High Court shows that the impost was by way of licence fee on the mining of certain minerals.	None
Regulation and development of mines and minerals was undertaken by the Central Legislation and therefore the power of the State Legislature under Entries 23 and 52 in List II got denuded in the field of regulation and development covered by the Central Legislation.	None
The Division Bench vide para 6 held it is therefore clear that to the extent the Central Act makes provision regarding the regulation and development of minerals, the powers of the State Legislatures under Entry 23 of List II stand curtailed The State Government had sought to defend the licence fee on the ground that it was in the nature of a tax and not a licence fee.	None
This plea has been specifically noted by the High Court and dealt with.	None
However, what is significant to note is the revelation, made by careful reading of the judgment, that provision for licence fee was made in the Central Legislation and licence fee was sought to be imposed by the State too.	None
In fact, the licence fee was a step trenching upon the field of regulation and therefore was liable to be struck down on this ground alone.	None
Yet, another reasoning which prevailed with the High Court was that Section 143 of the State Act, which was not inconsistent with the Central Act, was relied on by the State Government as conferring power on it to levy the impugned licence fee.	None
On that plea the High Court formed an opinion that on the framing of Section 143 of the State Act it did not express terms authorize a levy of fee or tax.	None
The High Court observed  It (Section 143) cannot also be construed as conferring such a power on the respondents to levy a tax or fee on mining, in view of the wellsettled and statutory construction that a Court construing a provision of law must presume that the intention of the authority in making it was not to exceed its power but to enact it validity.	None
The ratio of the decision of the Mysore High Court is that provision for licences and license fees, operation in the field or regulation of mines and minerals is not available to be made by State legislation  in view of the declaration in terms of Entry 54 in List I.	None
In our view, the decision by Mysore High Court cannot be read so widely as laying down the law that Unions power to regulate and control results in depriving the States of their power to levy tax or fee within their legislative competence without trenching upon the field of regulation and control.	None
There is a distinction between power to regulate and control and power to tax, the two being distinct and that difference has not been kept in view by the Mysore High Court.	None
(A diversion from main issue)	None
Royalty, if tax?	None
We would like to avail this opportunity for pointing out an error, attributable either to a stenographers devil or to sheer inadvertence, having crept into the majority judgment in India Cement Ltd.s case (supra).	None
The error in apparent and only needs a careful reading to detect.	None
We feel constrained  rather duty bound  to say so, lest a reading of the judgment containing such an error  just an error of one word  should continue to cause the likely embarrassment and have adverse effect on the subsequent judicial pronouncements which would follow India Cement Ltd.s case, feeling bound and rightly, by the said judgment having the force of pronouncement by sevenJudges Bench.	None
Para 34 of the report reads as under In the aforesaid view of the matter, we are of the opinion that royalty is a tax, and as such a cess on royalty being a tax on royalty, is beyond the competence of the State legislature because Section 9 of the Central Act covers the field and the State legislature is denuded of its competence under Entry 23 of List II.	None
In any event, we are of the opinion that cess on royalty cannot be sustained under Entry 49 of List II as being a tax on land.	None
Royalty on minerals rights is not a tax on land but a payment for the user of land.	None
(underlining by us)	None
In the first sentence the word royalty occurring in the expression  royalty is a tax, is clearly an error.	None
What the majority wished to say, and has in fact said, is  cess on royalty is a tax.	None
The correct words to be printed in the judgment should have been cess on royalty in place of royalty only.	None
The words cess on appear to have been inadvertently or erroneously omitted while typing the text of judgment.	None
This is clear from reading the judgment in its entirety.	None
Vide para 22 and 31, which precede para 34 above said, their Lordships have held that royalty is not a tax.	None
Even the last line of para 34 records royalty on mineral rights is not a tax on land but a payment for the user of land.	None
The very first sentence of the para records in quick succession as such a cess on royalty being a tax on royalty, is beyond the competence of the State Legislature What their Lordships have intended to record is that cess on royalty is a tax and as such a cess on royalty being a tax on royalty is beyond the competence of the State Legislature That makes correct and sensible reading.	None
A doubtful expression occurring in a judgment, apparently by mistake for inadvertence, ought to be read by assuming that the Court had intended to say only that which is correct according to the settled position of law, and the apparent error should be ignored, far from making any capital out of it, giving way to the correct expression which ought to be implied or necessarily read in the context, also having regard to what has been said a little before and a little after.	None
No learned Judge would consciously author a judgment which is selfinconsistent or incorporates passages repugnant to each other.	None
Vide para 22, their Lordships have clearly held that there is no entry in Schedule II which enables the State to impose a tax on royalty and, therefore, the State was incompetent to impose such a tax (cess).	None
The cess which has an incidence of an additional charge on royalty and not a tax on land, cannot apparently be justified as failing under Entry 49 in List II.	None
It is of significance for the issue before us, to determine the nature of royalty and whether it is a tax, and if not, then what it is.	None
Until the pronouncement of this Court in India Cement (supra), it has been the uniform and unanimous judicial opinion that royalty is not a tax.	None
First will refer to certain dictionaries oftcited in courts of law.	None
Words and Phrases, Permanent Edition (Vol. 37A, page 597)  	None
Royalty is the share of the produce reserved to owner for permitting another to exploit and use property.	None
The word royalty means compensation paid to landlord by occupier of land for species of occupation allowed by contract between them.	None
Royalty is a share of the product or profit (as of a mine, forest etc.)	None
reserved by the owner for permitting another to use his property.	None
Strouds Judicial Dictionary of Words and Phrases (Sixth Edition, 2000, Vol.3, page 2341)  	None
The word royalties signifies, in mining leases, that part of the reddendum which is variable, and depends upon the quantity of minerals gotten or the agreed payment to a patentee on every article made according to the patent.	None
Rights or privileges for which remuneration is payable in the form of a royalty.	None
Words and Phrases, Legally Defined (Third Edition, 1990, Vol. 4, page 112)  	None
A royalty, in the sense in which the word is used in connection with mining leases, is a payment to the lessor proportionate to the amount of the demised mineral worked within a specified period.	None
Whartons Law Lexicon (Fourteenth Edition, page 893)  Royalty, payment to a patentee by agreement on every article made according to his patent or to an another by a publisher on every copy of his book sold or to the owner of minerals for the right of working the same on every ton or other weight raised.	None
Mozley Whiteleys Law Dictionary (Eleventh Edition, 1993, page 243)  A pro rata payment to a grantor or lessor, on the working of the property leased, or otherwise on the profits of the grant of lease.	None
The word is especially used in reference to mines, patents and copyrights.	None
Prems Judicial Dictionary (1992, Vol. 2, page 1458)  royalties are payments which the Government may demand for the approprition of minerals, timber or other property belonging to the Government.	None
Two important features of royalty have to be noticed, they are, that the payment made for the privilege of removing the articles is in proportion to the quantity removed, and the basis of the payment is an agreement.	None
Blacks Law Dictionary (Seventh Edition, p.1330)  Royalty  A share of the product or profit from real property, reserved by the grantor of a mineral lease, in exchange for the lessees right to mine or drill on the land.	None
Mineral Royalty A right to a share of income from mineral production.	None
In D.K. Trivedi Sons and others vs. State of Gujarat others 1986 (Supp) SCC 20, a Bench of two learned Judges of this Court dealt with rent, royalty and dead rent and held as follows.	None
Rent is an integral part of the concept of a lease.	None
It is the consideration from the lessee to the lessor for the demise of the property to him.	None
In a mining lease the consideration usually moving from the lessee to the lessor is the rent of the areas leased (often called surface rent), dead rent and royalty.	None
Since the mining lease confers upon the lessee the right not merely to enjoy the property as under an ordinary lease but also to extract minerals from the land and to appropriate them for his own use or benefit, in addition to the usual rent for the area demised, the lessee is required to pay a certain amount in respect of the minerals extracted proportionate to the quantity so extracted.	None
Such payment is called royalty.	None
It may, however, be that the mine is not worked properly so as not to yield enough return to the lessor in the shape of royalty.	None
In order to ensure for the lessor a regular income, regardless of whether the mine is worked or not, a fixed amount is provided to be paid to him by the lessee.	None
This is called dead rent.	None
Dead rent is calculated on the basis of the area leased while royalty is calculated on the quantity of minerals extracted or removed.	None
Thus, while dead rent is a fixed return to the lessor, royalty is a return which varies with the quantity of minerals extracted or removed.	None
Since dead rent and royalty are both a return to the lessor in respect of the area leased, looked at from one point of view dead rent can be described as the minimum guaranteed amount of royalty payable to the lessor but calculated on the basis of the area leased, and not on the quantity of minerals extracted or removed.	None
In H.R.S. Murthy vs. Collector of Chittor, (1964) 6	None
SCR 666 , too the Constitution Bench of this Court had defined Royalty to mean the payment made for the materials or minerals won from the land.	None
The judicial opinion as prevailing amongst the High Courts may be noticed.	None
A Full Bench of the High Court of Orissa held in Laxmi Narayan Agarwalla and others vs. State of Orissa and others, AIR 1983 Orissa 210, Royalty is the payment made for the minerals extracted it is not tax.	None
In Surajdin Laxmanlal vs. State of M.P., Nagpur and others  AIR 1960 M.P. 129, a Division Bench of the High Court of Madhya Pradesh referred to the Whartons Law Lexicon and Mozley Whiteleys Law Dictionary and said  royalties are payments which the Government may demand for the appropriation of minerals, timber or other property belonging to the Government.	None
The High Court opined that there are two important features of royalty (i) the payment is in proportion to the quantity removed and (ii) the basis of payment is an agreement.	None
Drawing a distinction between royalty and tax, a Division Bench of the High Court of Punjab and Haryana High Court held in Dr. Shanti Saroop Sharma vs. Anr.	None
vs. State of Punjab and others (AIR 1969 Punjab and Haryana 79 as under if a person is merely in occupation of land which contains minor minerals, he is not liable to pay any royalty, but it is only when he holds a mining lease and by virtue of that extracts one or more minor minerals that he is called upon to pay royalty to the Government where the lease is in respect of the land in which minor minerals vest in the Government.	None
Royalty thus has its basis in the contract.	None
For payment to the owner of the minerals for the privilege of extracting the minor minerals computed on the basis of the quantity actually extracted and removed from the leased area.	None
It is more akin to rent or compensation payable to an owner by the occupier or lessee of land for its use or exploitation of the resources contained therein.	None
Merely because the provision with regard to royalty is made by virtue of the rules relating to the regulation of the mining leases and a uniform rate is prescribed, it does not follow that it is a compulsory exaction in the nature of tax or impost.	None
A Division Bench of Gujarat High Court in Saurashtra Cement and Chemical Industries Ltd., Ranavav vs. Union of India and Anr.  	None
AIR 1979 Gujarat 180, emphatically said  royalty may not be a fee	None
but it is not a tax.	None
It is a payment for the mineral which is removed or consumed by the holder of the mining lease.	None
The minerals themselves,  the property beneath the soil belong to the Union.	None
When the holder of a mining lease removes these minerals or consumes them, he can do so only on payment of its price or value.	None
Therefore, royalty is a share which the Union claims in the minerals which have been won from the soil by the lessee and which otherwise belong to it.	None
Royalty is a share in such minerals and not a tax in the form of a compulsory exaction.	None
It is not compulsory because anyone who applies for a mining lease to win minerals for being removed or consumed must pay its price.	None
If he does not want to pay the price, he may not apply for a mining lease.	None
Royalty which is a share of the owner of the minerals  the Union  won by the lessee from the soil with the authority of the Union can never be said to be an imposition on the holder of a mining lease.	None
We need not further multiply the authorities.	None
Suffice it to say that until the pronouncement in India Cement, nobody doubted the correctness of royalty not being a tax.	None
Such has been the position even subsequent to the pronouncement in India Cement.	None
In Inderjeet Singh Sial and another vs. Karam Chand Thapar and others (1995) 6 SCC 166 , a Bench of two learned judges held that  In its primary and natural sense royalty, in the legal world, is known as the equivalent or translation of jura regalia or jura regia.	None
Royal rights and prerogatives of a sovereign are covered thereunder.	None
In its secondary sense the word royalty would signify, as in mining leases, that part of the reddendum, variable though, payable in cash or kind, for rights and privileges obtained.	None
It is found in the clause of the deed by which the grantor reserves something to himself out of that which he grants.	None
It may even be a clause reserving rent in a lease, whereby the lesser reserves something for himself out of that which he grants.	None
In Ajit Singh vs. Union of India and others (1995 Supp.	None
(4) SCC 224, another Bench of two learned Judges held that the grant of mining lease involves grant of a privilege by the State.	None
In both these decisions India Cements is not noticed.	None
In Quarry Owners Association vs. State of Bihar and others (2000) 8 SCC 655 , a Bench of two learned Judges was faced with a submission, based on India Cement and subsequent decisions following it, that royalty is a tax.	None
The learned Judges found it difficult to accept the concept but tried to wriggle out of the situation by observing  royalty includes the price for the consideration of parting with the right and privilege of the owner, namely, the State Government who owns the mineral.	None
In other words, the royalty dead rent, which a lessee or licensee pays, includes the price of the minerals which are the property of the State.	None
Both royalty and dead rent are integral parts of a lease.	None
Thus, it does not constitute usual tax as commonly understood but includes return for the consideration for parting with its property.	None
In India Cement (vide para 31, SCC) decisions of four High Courts holding Royalty is not tax have been noted without any adverse comment.	None
Rather, the view seems to have been noted with tacit approval.	None
Earlier (vide para 21, SCC) the connotative meaning of royalty being share in the produce of land has been noted.	None
But for the first sentence (in para 34, SCC) which we find to be an apparent error, no where else has the majority judgment held royalty to be a tax.	None
How the above noted inadvertent error in India Cement has resulted into throwing on the loop line the movement of later case law on this point may be noticed.	None
In State of M.P. vs. Mahalaxmi Fabric Mills Ltd. and others  1995 Supp.	None
(1) SCC 642 (decision by a Bench of three learned Judges), and	None
Saurashtra Cement and Chemicals Industries and Anr. etc. etc. vs. Union of India and others (2001) 1 SCC 91 (decision by a Bench of two learned Judges) para 34 (from SCC) in India Cement has been quoted verbatim and dealt with.	Facts
In Mahalaxmi Fabric Mills Ltd. and otherss case (supra), the court noticed several dictionaries defining royalty and also the decisions of High Courts available and stated that traditionally speaking royalty is an amount which is paid under contract of lease by the lessee to the lessor, namely, the State Governments concerned and it is commensurate with the quality of minerals extracted.	None
But then (vide para 12), the Court felt bound by the view taken in India Cement, reiterated in Orissa Cement, to hold that royalty is a tax.	None
The point that there was apparently a typographical error in para 34 in India Cement was specifically raised but was rejected.	None
In Saurashtra Cement and Chemicals Industries and anr.	None
(supra) too the Court felt itself bound by the decision in Mahalaxmi Fabric Mills Ltd. and others (supra), backed by India Cement and therefore held royalty to be tax.	None
We have clearly pointed out the said error, as we are fully convinced in that regard and feel ourselves obliged constitutionally, legally and morally to do so, lest the said error should cause any further harm to the trend of jurisprudential thought centering around the meaning of royalty.	None
We hold that royalty is not tax.	Reasoning
Royalty is paid to the owner of land who may be a private person and may not necessarily be State.	Reasoning
A private person owning the land is entitled to charge royalty but not tax.	Reasoning
The lessor receives royalty as his income and for the lessee the royalty paid is an expenditure incurred.	Reasoning
Royalty cannot be tax.	Reasoning
We declare that even in India Cement it was not the finding of the Court that royalty is a tax.	Reasoning
A statement caused by an apparent typographical or inadvertent error in a judgment of the Court should not be misunderstood as declaration of such law by the Court.	Reasoning
We also record our express dissent with that part of the judgment in Mahalaxmi Fabric Mills Ltd. and others which says (vide para 12 of SSC report) that there was no typographical error in India Cement and that the said conclusion that royalty is a tax logically flew from the earlier paragraphs of the judgment.	Reasoning
Interrelationship of Schedule I Entry 54 and Schedule II Entry 23 With the abovesaid reflection of ours on clarifying India Cement, clarification now we proceed to examine the interrelationship of Schedule	None
I Entry 54 and Schedule II Entry 23 which have been quoted and reproduced in the earlier part of this judgment.	None
Conflict in Entries (in the three lists in Seventh Schedule)	None
The analysis of decide cases as made by eminent constitutional jurist H.M. Seervai in his work on Constitutional Law of India (Fourth Silver Jubilee Edition, Vol. 3) is apposite.	None
Vide para 22.168, he states  	None
In Gov.Gen. in Council vs. Madras, 1945 FCR 179, the Privy Council laid down important principles for interpreting apparently conflicting legislative entries in general, and apparently conflicting tax entries in particular.	None
The Privy Council held, first, that though a tax in List I (e.g. a duty of excise) and a tax in List II (e.g. a tax on the sale of goods) of the Government of India Act, 1935, may overlap, in fact there would be no overlapping in law, if the taxes were separate and distinct imposts secondly, that the machinery of tax collection did not affect the real nature of a tax.	None
Another principle for reconciling apparently conflicting tax entries follows from the fact that a tax has two elements the person, thing or activity on which the tax is imposed, and the amount of the tax.	None
The amount may be measured in many ways but decided cases establish a clear distinction between the subject matter of a tax and the standard by which the amount of tax is measured.	Reasoning
These two elements are described as the subject of a tax and the measure of a tax.	Reasoning
In D.G. Gouse vs. Kerala  (1980) 2 SCC 410 , which is considered later, the above passage was quoted with approval by the Supreme Court as stating precisely the two elements involved in almost all tax cases, namely, the subject of a tax and the measure of a tax.	None
It is necessary to examine the scheme underlying the Seventh Schedule of the Constitution.	None
We are relieved of the need of embarking upon any maiden voyage in this direction in view of the availability of a Constitution Bench decision in M.P.V. Sundararamier Co. vs. The State of Andhra Pradesh and Anr.	None
(1958)	None
SCR 1422.	None
Venkatarama Aiyar, J., speaking for the Constitution Bench, traced the history of legislations preceding the Constitution, analysed the scheme underlying the division of legislative powers between the Centre and the States and then succinctly summed up the quintessence of the analysis.	None
It was held, inter alia In List I, Entries 1 to 81 mention the several matters over which Parliament has authority to legislate.	None
Entries 82 to 92 enumerate the taxes which could be imposed by a law of Parliament.	None
An examination of these two groups of Entries shows that while the main subject of legislation figures in the first group a tax in relation thereto is separately mentioned in the second.	None
In List II, Entries 1 to 44 form one group mentioning the subjects on which the States could legislate.	None
Entries 45 to 63 in that List from another group, and they deal with taxes.	None
Taxation is not intended to be comprised in the main subject in which it might on an extended construction be regarded as included, but is treated as a distinct matter for purposes of legislative competence.	None
And this distinction is also manifest in the language of Art.	None
248, Cls.	None
(1) and (2) and of Entry 97 in List I of the Constitution.	None
Under the scheme of the Entries in the Lists, taxation is regarded as a distinct matter and is separately set out.	None
The entries in the Legislative Lists must be construed breadly and not narrowly or in a pedantic manner.	None
The entries in the two Lists  List I and II  must be construed, if possible, so as to avoid conflict.	Reasoning
Faced with a suggested conflict between entries in List I and List II, what has first to be decided in whether there is any conflict.	None
If there is none, the question of application of the nonobstante clause subject to does not arise.	None
And, if there be conflict, the correct approach to the question is to see whether it was possible to effect a reconciliation between the two Entries so as to avoid a conflict and overlapping.	None
Illustration If it is possible to construe Entry 42 in List I as not including tax on interstate sales it should be so construed and the power to levy such tax must be held to be included in Entry 54 in List II (Entries as they existed preForty Second Amendment, 1976) (See Governor General in Council vs. Province of Madras  AIR 1945 PC 98 , and Province of Madras vs. Bodder Paidenna and Sons  AIR 1942 FC 33)	None
In the event of a dispute arising it should be determined by applying the doctrine of pith and substance to find out whether between two Entries assigned to two different legislatures the particular subject of the legislation fails within the ambit of the one or the other.	Reasoning
Where there is a clear and irreconcilable conflict of jurisdiction between the Centre and a provincial legislature it is the law of the Centre that must prevail.	Reasoning
(underlining by us)	None
Referring to M.P.V. Sundararamier Co. (supra) Sabyasachi Mukharji, J., (as his Lordship then was) speaking for six out of the seven Judges constituting the Bench in Synthetics and Chemicals Ltd. and others vs. State of U.P. and others  (1990) 1 SCC 109 held that under the constitutional scheme of division of powers in the Seventh Schedule, there are separate entries pertaining to taxation and other laws.	None
A tax cannot be levied under a general entry.	None
The abovesaid principles continue to hold that the field and have been followed in cases after cases.	None
General power of Regulation and Control does not include power of taxation.	None
One thing, which too is well settled by a series of decisions is that the power of regulation and control is separate and distinct from the power of taxation.	None
How this principle has been applied in myriad situations may be illustratively noticed.	None
The Constitution Bench in The HingirRampur Coal Co. Ltd. and others vs. The State of Orissa and others etc.  (1961) 2	Reasoning
SCR 537 , was faced with a challenge to the constitutional validity of the Orissa Mining Areas Development Fund Act, 1952.	None
The petitionercompany was engaged in producing and selling coal excavated from its collieries at Rampur in the State of Orissa.	None
