Durga Das Basu in his celebated work Comparative Federalism at pp.	None
175176 states The strong Central bias has indeed been a boon to keep India together when we find the separatist forces of communalism, linguism and scramble for power playing havoc notwithstanding all the devices of Central control, even after more than three decades of the working of the Constitution.	None
It also shows that the States are not really functioning as agents of the Union Government of under the directions of the latter, for then, events like those in Assam (over the language problem) or in Punjab (pp. 115ff., ante) could not have taken place at all.	None
But, by reason of such centralizing trends.	None
federalism cannot be said to be dead in India.	None
A radical change in the background has taken place since 1967.	None
So long as the Union and all the States in India were under the rule of oneParty under the strong leadership of a growing personality such as Pandit Jawahar Lal or Mrs. Gandhi, there could hardly arise any tussle between the Union and the States which could not be settled by the Party leadership at Delhi, and, thus, Indian federalismcame to work almost as a unitary system.	None
But in 1967, different parties came to power in a number of States, so that they would naturally refuse to act as dictated by the Party in power at Delhi.	None
The frequent resort to the extraordinary power under Art.	None
356 to keep recalcitrant State politics under Union control, the abuse of Governors powers in some cases, and the like, have accelerated the forces of separatism.	None
In such a situation, which is prevailing till the time of this writing in 1986, the question of State powers under the provisions of the existing Constitution, as well as the question of their revision by amendment of the Constitution, are bound to raise their head and agitations over these questions have led to the constitution of the Sarkaria Commission (1983) to examine and revise, if necessary, the Centre State relations under the Constitution.	None
In the said treatise at page 178 quoting Dicey, Law of the Constitution, 10th Ed.	None
p. 164, the learned author states There are, according to Dicey, three essential legal features in a federal Constitution, namely, Supremacy of a written Constitution Distribution of powers amongst the various organs of the federation and of the regional units of the federation, by the provisions of that Constitution and Judicial review or enforcement of that supreme Constitution as law.	None
If these legal features are present in the Indian Constitution, it would be immaterial to a lawyer whether academicians would classify it at quas of a unitary constitution which subsidiary federal teatures, of the like.	None
In his book, Central power in the Australian Commonwealth, Cassell, London.	None
Sir Robert Menzies states My central purpose has been to demonstrate a great truth about the study of a federal Constitution.	None
That truth is that although it is a sound rule by go back to the language of the constitution  melius est petere fonts quam sectar rivulos  it is a mistake to think that a Constitution is something rigid and inflexible, to be interpreted like any ordinary statute, to have a meaning fixed for all time, I have defended legalism as something inherent in federalism.	None
But it is not inconsistent with the legalistic approach to recognize that a written Constitution is an expressed scheme of government designed to give a basic structure in a changing world not designed to inhibit growth in a growing world.	None
nor to make the contemporary world subject to the political, social or economic ideas of a bygone age.	None
The doctrine or federalism in the Indian context would mean proper and effective interpretation of the Constitution in respect whereof political or economic views have no role to play.	None
Fields of legislation carved out under Chapter I of Part XI clearly spells out that in more important matter and the Parliament will have greater control thereover.	None
Tilt in favour of the Central is required to be construed having regard to the importance of the subject matter of Parliamentary legislation and the impact and practical effect of the in road of the State Laws entrenching upon the legislative field occupied by the Parliament.	None
It would, therefore, not be correct for the superior courts to advocate the theory that while interpreting the Constitution, courts should lean in favour of the State.	None
Federal character of the Union of States in India do not support the said theory.	None
Legislative Field	None
The principles required to be deduced as regard field of legislation, may not be much in dispute.	None
The question, however, it that of its application.	None
Before analyzing the relevant provisions, we may have an overview of the constitutional scheme in this behalf.	None
Articles 245 and 246 of the Constitution of India read with Seventh Schedule and Legislative Lists contained therein prescribe the extent of legislative competence of Parliament and State Legislatures.	None
Parliament has exclusive power to make laws with respect of any of the matters enumerated in List I in the Seventh Schedule.	None
Similarly, State Legislatures have exclusive power to make laws in respect of any of the matters enumerated in List II.	None
Parliament and State Legislatures both have legislative power to make laws with respect to any matter enumerated in List III, the Concurrent List.	None
The various entries in the three Lists are fields of legislation.	None
They are designed to define and delimit the respective areas of legislative competence of the Union and State Legislatures.	None
Since legislative subjects cannot always be divided into water tight compartments some overlappings between List I, II and III of the Seventh Schedule is inevitable.	None
Hence, though the State Legislature has exclusive power with respect to the subjects specified in List II, some of the Entries in List II specifically make the State power subject to any law made by Parliament under the specified Entry in List I. Article 245 of the Constitution of India empowers the Parliament not only to make laws for the whole or any part of the territory of India but also indicate that no law made by the Parliament shall be deemed to be invalid on the ground that it would have extraterritorial operation.	None
Clause (1) of Article 246 of the Constitution of India confers exclusive legislative power upon the Parliament with respect to any of the matters enumerated in List I in the Seventh Schedule whereas in terms of clause (2) thereof, the Legislature of any State also have power to make laws with respect to any of the matters enumerated in List III in the Seventh Schedule, subject of course to the legislative competence of the Parliament as contained in clause (3) thereof.	Reasoning
The power of the State Legislature in terms of clause (3) of Article 246 is subject to clauses (1) and (2) in relation to the matters enumerated in List II in the Seventh Schedule.	None
In Union and State relations under the Indian Constitution by M.C. Setalvad, upon noticing the expressions used in different clauses of Article 246, it is stated In the United States and in Canada, judicial decisions have established that, where a federal law or a dominion law conflicts with a State law on the same subject, the relevant federal or dominion law must prevail.	None
The same position has been achieved by an express provision in section 109 of the Commonwealth of Australia Act.	None
In the Indian Constitution, this is sought to be achieved in part by the language of Article 246.	None
The purpose of the provisions which we have set out in Article 246(1), (2) and (3), is clearly to carve out not only two exclusive legislative fields for the Union and the States and a further field in which both the general and the regional governments can operate, but also to provide by the language used in each of three clauses of the Article that the legislative power of the Union in List I is predominant.	None
That power is exercisable notwithstanding anything in clauses (2) and (3) of Article 246.	None
The concurrent Union power of legislation conferred by clause (2) of Article 246 is exercisable notwithstanding anything in clause (3) which deals with the exclusive legislative power of the State.	None
But the States concurrent legislative power is subject to clause (1), which deals with the exclusive Union power of legislation.	None
The States legislative power in the field carved out for it by List II is again exercisable subject to clauses (1) and (2), which deal with the Union power and the Concurrent power, the first vested exclusively in the Union and the second in both the Union and the State, Emphasis supplied The Construction makers found the need for power sharing devices between the Central and the State having regard to the imperatives of the States security and stability and, thus, propelled the thrust towards centralisation by using non obstants clause under article 246 so as to see that the federal supremacy is achieved.	None
A perusal of the provisions of entries in List II would show that there are 17 entries in List II (Entries 1, 2, 12, 13, 17, 22, 23, 24, 26, 27, 32,33, 37, 50 54, 57 64) which are one way or the other subject to either provisions of Entries in List I and or List III or subject to laws made by Parliament.	None
There are four models of entries to that effect.	None
Eight Entries (2, 13, 17, 22, 23, 24, 33, 54) out of the aforesaid 17 entries have been made subject to the provisions of Entries in List I. Three Entries (26, 27, 57) have been made subject to provisions of Entries in List III.	None
Four Entries (1, 12, 32, 63) out of the aforesaid 17 entries have been given power to the State Legislatures to make laws on subjects other than those specified in List I and or dealt with by law mae by Parliament.	None
Only two Entries (27 50) have been made subject to the provisions of any law made by Parliament matter not enumerated in the Concurrent List of the State List.	None
Article 253 of the Constitution of India reads thus Legislation for giving effect to international agreements  Notwithstanding anything in the foregoing provisions of this Chapter, Parliament has power to make any law for the whole or any part of the territory of India for implementing any treaty, agreement or convention with any other country or countries or any decision made at any international conference, association or other body.	None
It can be seen that Art.	None
253 contains nonobstante clause.	None
Art.	None
253, thus, operates notwithstanding anything contained in Art.	None
245 and Art.	None
246.	None
Art.	None
246 confers power on the Parliament to enact laws with respect to matters enumerated in List I of the Seventh Schedule to the Constitution.	None
Entries 10 to 21 of List I of the Seventh Schedule pertain to International Law.	None
In making any law under any of these entries, parliament is required to keep Art.	None
51 in mind.	None
Article 253 of Constitution provides that while giving effect to an international treaty, the Parliament assumes the role of the State Legislature and once the same is done the power of the State is denuded.	None
Notwithstanding the fact that great care with which the various entries in the three lists have been framed on some rare occasions it may be found that one or the other field is not covered by these entries.	None
The makers for our Constitution have, in such a case, take care by conferring power to legislate on such residuary subjects upon the Union Parliament including taxation by reason of Article 248 of the Constitution.	None
We may notice that in the Government of India Act, 1935 no provision of the nature of Entry 97 in List I existed.	None
In terms of Section 104 thereof the Governor General could empower either the Dominion Legislature or a Provincial Legislature to enact a law with respect to any matter not enumerated in any of the Lists in the Seventh Schedule to the Act, including a law imposing a tax not mentioned in any such list and the executive authority of the Dominion or of the Province, as the case may be, shall extend to the administration of any law so made, unless the Governor General otherwise directs.	None
In Constitution of India however, such a residuary power has expressly been conferred on the Parliament.	None
Once it is held that the State lacks legislative competence for imposition of tax on any of the subject, indisputably the Parliament alone will have legislative competence therefor.	None
Case Laws Re Legislative Competence Observation made by this Court in S.R. Choudhuri v. State of Punjab, (2001) 7 SCC 126 , in this regard, is apposite Constitutional provisions are required to be understood and interpreted with an objectoriented approach.	None
A Constitution must not be construed in a narrow and pedantic sense.	None
The words used may be general in terms but, their full import and true meaning, has to be appreciated considering the true context in which the same are used and the purpose which they seek to achieve.	None
In Attorney General for India Vs. Amratal Prajivandas, (1994) 5 SCC 54 , the Smugglers and Foreign Exchange Manipulators (Forfeiture of Property) Act, 1976 (SAFEMA) made by Parliament was challenged inter alia as lacking legislative competence.	None
The Constitution Bench of nineJudges relying on Union of India Vs.	None
Shri Harbhajan Singh Dhillon (1971) 2 SCC 779 observed as under Be that as it may, it is not necessary to pursue this line of reasoning since we are in total agreement with the approach evolved in Union of India v. H.S. Dhillon  a decision by a Constitution bench of seven Judges.	None
The test evolved in the said decision is this in short Where the legislative competence of Parliament to enact a particular statute is questioned, one must look at the several entries in List II to find out (applying the well known principles in this behalf) whether the said statute is relatable to any of those entries.	None
If the statute does not relate to any of the entries in List II, no further inquiry is necessary.	None
It must be held that Parliament is competent to enact that statute whether by virtue of the entries in List I and List III or by virtue of Article 248 read with Entry 97 of List I.	None
We may at this juncture also notice the decision of this Court in Naga Peoples Movement of Human Rights Vs. Union of India, AIR 1998 SC 431 which states While examining the legislative competence of Parliament to make a law what is required to be seen is whether the subjectmatter falls in the State List which Parliament cannot enter.	None
If the law does not fall in the State List, Parliament would have legislative competence to pass the law by virtue of the residuary powers under Article 248 read with Entry 97 of the Union List and it would not be necessary to go into the question whether it falls under any entry in the Union List or the Concurrent List.	None
See Union of India v. H.S. Dhillon, (1972) 2	None
SCR 33 at pp. 61 and 6768 (AIR 1972 SC 1061 at pp. 107475 and 1078)	None
S.P. Mittal v. Union of India, (1983) 1	None
SCR 729 at pp. 769770	None
AIR 1983 SC 1 at pp. 1819) and Kartar Singh v. State of Punjab, (1994) 3 SCC 569 at pp.	None
629630.	None
What is, therefore, required to be examined is whether the subjectmatter of the Central Act falls in any of the entries in the State List.	None
Yet again in Synthetic Chemicals Ltd. vs. State of U.P. AIR 1990 SC 1927 , it has been held It has also to be borne in mind that where division of powers and jurisdiction in a federal Constitution is the scheme, it is desirable to read the Constitution in harmonious way.	None
In State of A.P. Vs. K. Purushotham Reddy Ors. reported in JT 2003 (3) SC 15 , it was held the conflict in legislative competence of the Parliament and the State Legislatures having regard to Article 246 of the Constitution of India must be viewed in the light of the decisions of this Court which in no uncertain terms state that each Entry has to be interpreted in a broad manner.	None
Both the parliamentary legislation as also the State legislation must be considered in such a manner so as to uphold both of them and only in case where it is found that both cannot coexist, the State Act may be declared ultra vires In India Cement.	None
(supra), it is stated It is well settled that widest amplitude should be given to the language of these entries, but some of these entries in different lists or in the same list may overlap and sometimes may also appear to be in direct conflict with each other.	None
Then, it is the duty of the court to find out its true intent and purpose and to examine a particular legislation in its pith and substance to determine whether it fits in one or the other of the lists.	None
In Bharat Coking Coal Vs. State of Bihar (1990 4 SCR 557, it has been held No doubt under Entry 23 of List II, the State legislature has power to make law but that power is subject to Entry 54 of List I with respect to the regulation and development of mines and minerals.	None
As discussed earlier the State legislature is denuded of power to make laws on the subject in view of Entry 54 of List I and the Parliamentary declaration made under S. 2 of the Act.	None
The decisions of this Court, therefore, also lead to the conclusion that in case the State for one reason or the other lacks legislative competence, the court must proceed on the basis that Parliament alone has the legislative competence and it would not be permissible to uphold the State Act by leaning in favour of the State or by giving a broader meaning to the entry in List II relating to the subject matter of legislation.	None
Pith and Substance Doctrine of pith and substance, however, is taken recourse to when examining the constitutionality of an Act with respect to competing legislative competence of the Parliament and the State Legislature qua the subject matter.	None
Incidental entrenchment however is permissible.	None
In D.C. G.M. Co. Ltd.	None
Vs. Union of India.	None
AIR 1983 SC 937 , it has been held When a law is impugned on the ground that it is ultra vires the powers of the legislature which enacted it, what has to be ascertained is the true character of the legislation.	None
To do that one must have regard to the enactment as a whole, to its objects and to the scope and effect of its provisions.	Reasoning
to resolve the controversy if it becomes necessary to ascertain to which entry in the three Lists, the legislation is referable, the court has evolved the doctrine of pith and substance.	None
If in pith and substance, the legislation falls within one entry or the other but some portion of the subjectmatter of the legislation incidentally trenches upon the might enter a field under another List, then it must be held to be valid in its entirely, even though it might incidentally trench on matters which are beyond its competence.	None
In Ishwari Khetan Sugar Mills Ltd.	None
Vs.	Decision
State of U.P. AIR 1980 SC 1955 , it was held When validity of a legislation is challenged on the ground of want of legislative competence and it becomes necessary to ascertain to which entry in the three lists the legislation is referable to, the court has evolved the theory of pith and substance.	None
If in pith and substance a legislation falls within one entry or the other but some portion of the subjectmatter of the legislation incidentally trenches upon and might enter a field under another list, the Act as a whole would be valid notwithstanding such incidental trenching.	None
The question which, therefore, is required to be posed and answered is as to whether both the Acts can stand together or not.	None
While determining the question as to whether there exists any conflict, the real test would be as to whether both the legislations covering the field can stand together.	None
for the purpose of determination thereof, it may be necessary to look to the legislative history as also the decision of this Court.	None
In Kartar Singh Vs.	None
State of Punjab, (1994) (3) scc 569 this Court held In order to ascertain the pith and substance of the impugned enactments, the preamble, Statement of Objects and Reasons, the legal significance and the intendment of the provisions of these Acts, their scope and the nexus with the object that these Acts seek to subserve must be objectively examined in the background of the totality of the series of events Ascertainment of pith and substance of synonymous to ascertainment of true nature and character of the legislative competence necessitated for the purpose of determining whether it is a legislation with respect of one of the matters of the list.	None
Human expression and fallibility of legal draftsmanship cannot be lost sight of.	None
The principles of pith and substance is, thus, required to be applied only in approp4riate cases.	None
Analysis Re Legislative Field In the economic front, the country has to compete with the developed countries.	None
Global competition has reached such a stage that despite adequate production of coal and steel, the same are imported from other countries in India.	None
In the international markets also the question of import is going up as compared to export.	None
The manner in which the revenue is collected by the Centre and distributed to States falls for consideration by the appropriate constitutional authorities in terms of the provisions of the Constitution.	None
It is not correct to say that while interpreting the legislative field the country in case of conflict would lean in favour of the State keeping in view the fact that taxes under different heads are collected by the Centre and a part of revenue is made available to the States from time to time.	None
This Court is not concerned as to whether the Centre consumes the lions share of revenue or the same is subject matter of criticism at the hands of the State of financial observers.	None
Such an approach would not only run counter to the doctrine of federalism with a strong Centre but in the long run would prove to be counterproductive.	None
India is a signatory to various international treaties and covenants and being a party to WTO and GATT, it is obligated to fulfill its transnational obligations.	None
If for the purpose of giving effect to the international treaties, it in exercise of its power under Article 253 of the Constitution of India had taken over the legislative field occupied by List II of the Seventh schedule of the Constitution, no exception thereto can be taken.	None
While doing so, the Central Government shall give effect to the will of the makers of the Constitution and would not act contrary thereto or inconsistent therewith.	None
The legislative fields of the Union and the State very from country to country depending upon the requirement of the situation in which such provisions are made.	None
Although a lot can be said on the subject, keeping in view the fact that our job is confined to interpretation of the legislative entries visavis the Parliamentary and Legislative Acts, it may not be necessary to do so.	None
But suffice however it to point out that when such an approach is adopted, we would be more prone to committing errors.	None
We must proceed on the basis that neither the Union nor the State is supreme on the Constitution, as both the Union and the State will have to trace their power from the provisions of the Constitution.	None
We should treat the subject with caution and circumspection.	None
The interpretive principles whether learning in favour of the Union or the State may, in certain situations, depend upon the subject matter of legislation, the importance thereof and its effect and impact within the outside the country.	None
Both mineral and tea deserve more control only by the Union having regard to their importance in national economy.	None
In ascertaining the subject matter, or the scope or purpose of the legislation, the Court is entitled to give due regard to its economic effect.	None
(See The King Vs. Barger (1908) 6 CLR 41 and AttorneyGeneral for Alberta Vs. Attorney General for Canada (1939) AC at pp. 130132).	None
The aforementioned decisions have been referred to in The State of South Australia and Another Vs.	None
the Commonwealth and Another.	None
(1942) 65 C.L.R. 373.	None
Distribution of taxes by the Central Government in favour of the State Government is of no moment in the instant cases as the entire royalty fixed by the Central Government in terms of the 1957 Act is payable to the States.	None
The Union government has nothing to do therewith.	None
If the Constitution as a living organ is not interpreted having regard to the intention of the constitution makers and in case of conflict in the legislative field contained in List I and List II, if an interpretation that leans in the favour of the State is adopted without reference to the subject matter thereof of national interest, the some would be subject to judicial vagaries which cannot be countenanced.	None
The impairment of States economic interest is of no moment even if Parliament had taken over the entire legislative field by enacting Acts in terms of Entry 52 of 54 of List I. As noticed by Brother Lohoti, J in South Eastarn Coalfields Limited Vs.	None
State of M.P. and Ors. 2003 (7) Supreme 539	None
the rate of royalty has been enhanced by the Central Government from Rs. 6.5 per ton to Rs. 120/ per ton.	None
All other states have accepted the same.	None

It may not be necessary for us to delve deep into the matter as to whether there exists a distinction between a general subject of legislation and taxation has such a question does not directly arise for consideration.	None
It may only be of some academic interest.	None
It is, however, trite that there is nothing in the Constitution to debar the Parliament to legislate under Entry 54 read with Entry 97 of the List I of the Seventh Schedule of the Constitution.	None
However, recourse to the residuary power must be taken as a last resort i.e. only when all the entries in the three lists are absolutely exhousted, that is to say, if the subject matter is beyond comprehension of the entries contained in the aforementioned three lists.	None
It is trite that when two interpretations are possible resort to the residuary power may not be taken recourse to.	None
But it is also trite that the entries have to be given a liberal construction irrespective of the fact that as to whether they are in List I or List II.	None
See South Eastern Coalfields (supra).	None
There cannot be any doubt whatsoever that for the said purpose, the main object as also the scope and purport of the Central legislation visavis the State legislation must be kept in mind and, thus, there cannot by any question of examining the same with joundiced eyes.	None
With the greatest respect, in Indian context it is difficult to follow Morey Vs. Doud (1957) 354 US 457 wherein Frankfurter, J. says The Courts have only the power to destroy, not to reconstruct.	None
The Courts in India generally leans in favour of upholding the constitutionality of the statute whether enacted by the State Legislature or the Parliament.	None
In this context, reference may be made to the decisions of this Court in Indian Handicraft Emporium Vs. Union of India (2003) 7 SCC 589 and Balram Kumawat Vs. Union of India (2003) 7 SCC 628 , wherein vires of Wild Life Protection Act has been upheld by applying the principles of Purposive Construction.	None

Union of India AIR 1981 SC 2138 in which reference of Morey (supra) has been made while judging the constitutionality of Special Bearer Bonds (immunities and Exemptions) Ordinance, this Court rejected the argument that the said ordinance is immoral stating It was then contended that the Act is unconstitutional as it offends against morality by according to dishonest assessee who have evaded payment of tax,immunities and exemptions which are denied to honest taxpayers.	None
Those who have broken the law and deprived the State of its legitimate dues fare given benefits and concessions placing them at an advantage over those who have observed the law and paid the taxes due from them and this, according to the petitioners, is clearly immoral and unwarranted by the Constitution.	None
We do not think this contention can be sustained.	None
It is necessary to remember that we are concerned here only with the constitutional validity of the Act and not with its morality.	None
It is, however, wellsettled that although both the Union and the State derive their power from the same Constitution, the States would not have any legal right as against the overriding powers of the Union, because of a general theory of paramountcy or superiority of the Union.	None
The Union can claim overriding powers or superior powers over the State in certain situation because the Constitution itself provides therefor.	None

I It may be that the interpretation of the legislative fields of the State List and Union List should be construed in deference to the extent of declaration made by the Parliament in terms of Entry 52 List I of the Constitution of India.	None

In P. Kannadasam and Others Vs.	None
State of T.N. and Others (1996) 5 SCC 670 , P.P. Jeevan Reddy, J. speaking for the Bench held The fifth contention of the learned counsel for the appellantspetitioners is equally misconceived.	None

Section 9, learned counsel submitted, is one of the facets of the extent of denudation.	None

This contention assumes that notwithstanding the declaration contained in Section 2 of the MMRD Act, the States still retain the power to lavy taxes upon minerals over and above those prescribed by the MMRD Act and that a fresh declaration is called for whenever such subsisting power of the State is sought to be further encroached upon.	None

It is for this reason that the State enactments were declared incompetent insofar as they purported to levy taxes cesses on minerals.	None
The denudation of the State is not partial.	None
It is total.	None
They cannot levy any tax or cess on minerals so long as the declaration in Section 2 stands.	None
Once the denudation is total, there is no occasion or necessity for any further declaration of denudation or, for that matter, for repeated declarations of denudation.	None
Emphasis supplied We are not oblivious of the fact that the said decision has been overruled by a threeJudge Bench in District Mining Officer and Others Vs.	None

Different considerations may arise as regard interpretation of different entries keeping in view the lists in which they belong.	None
The Court may have to look from a different angle in a case where it relates to interpretation of conflicting entries in List I visavis List II and List II visavis List II.	None
In a case where both the State Act and the Central Act have been enacted in terms of List III, the question of repugnancy as envisaged under Article 254 would arise.	None
In that type of cases, it is wellsettled that in absence of Presidential assent, the Parliamentary Act would prevail.	None

The Court in such a situation would compare the provisions of the two Acts so as to find out as to whether the entire field has been occupied by the Parliamentary Act or not.	None
The situation may, however, be different where there is no apparent conflict between an entry in List II and one in List I.	None

Sabharwal, J. Proceeded to uphold the market fee levied on tobacco on the basis that Parliament was not competent to pass legislation in respect of sale of agricultural produce of tobacco covered by Entry 52 of the Union List under which the Parliament can legislative only in respect of the industries, namely, the process of manufacture of production.	None
It was in that premise held that the activity regarding sale of raw tobacco as provided in the Tobacco Board Act would not be regarded as industry.	None
Ruma Pal J. in her concurrent judgment observed To sum up the word Industry for the purposes of Entry 52 of List I has been firmly confined by Tika Ramji to the process of manufacturer or production only.	None
Subsequent decisions including those of other Constitution Benches have reaffirmed that Tika Ramji case authoritatively defined the word industry  to mean the process of manufacture or production and that it does not include the raw materials used in the industry or the distribution of the products of the industry.	None
Given the constitutional framework, and the weight of judicial authority it is not possible to accept an argument canvassing a wider meaning of the word industry.	None
Whatever the word may mean in any other context, it must be understood in the Constitutional context as meaning manufacture or production.	None
Pattnaik, J., however, for himself and Bharucha, J. (as the learned Chief Justices then were) observed In view of the aforesaid rules of interpretation as well as the Constitution Bench decision referred to above, it is difficult for us to accept the contention of Mr. Dwivedi that the word industry in Entry 52 of List I should be given a restricted meaning, so as to exclude from its purview the subject of legislation coming within entry 27 or Entry 14 of List II.	None
Bearing in mind the constitutional schemd of supremacy of Parliament, the normal rule of interpretation of an Entry in any of the lists in the Seventh Schedule of the Constitution, the object of taking over the control of the tobacco industry by the Parliament, on making a declaration as required under Entry 52 of List I and on examining the different provisions of the Tobacco Board Act, we see no justification for giving a restricted meaning to the expression industry in Entry 52 of List I, nor do we find any justification in the contention of the counsel appearing for the States and also different Market Committees that the provisions contained in Tobacco Board Act dealing with the growing of tobacco as well as making provisions for sale and purchase of tobacco, must be held to be beyond the legislative competence of Parliament, as it does not come within the socalled narrow meaning of the expression industry on the ground that otherwise it would denude the State Legislature of its power to make law dealing with markets under Entry 28, dealing with agriculture under Entry 14 and dealing with goods under Entry 27 List II.	None
Such an approach of interpretation in our considered opinion would be against the very scheme of the constitution and supremacy of Parliament and such an approach towards interpreting the power sharing devices in relation to entries in List I and List II would be against the trust towards centralisation.	None
In our considered opinion, therefore, the word industry in Entry 52 of List I should not be given any restricted meaning and should be interpreted in a manner so as to enable the Parliament to make law in relation to the subject matter which is declared and whose control has been taken over to bring within its sweep any ancillary matter, which can be said to be reasonably included within the power and which may be incidental to the subject of legislation, so that Parliament would be able to make an effective law.	None
So constructed and on examining different provisions of the Tobacco Board Act, we do not find any lack of legislative competence with Parliament so as to enact any of the provisions contained in the said Act, the Act in question having been enacted by Parliament on a declaration being made of taking over of the control of the Tobacco industry by the Union and the Act being intended for the development of the said industry.	None
Even the majority opinion in ITC.	None
Ltd. (supra) would not come on the way of giving a broad interpretation of tea or mineral.	None

if a restricted meaning is given to Entries 52, 54 and 97 of List I and a broad meaning is given to Entries 5, 23, 24, 49 and 50 of List II of the Seventh Schedule of the Constitution of India, the same may result in incongruity inasmuch as thereby the goal and object of the Constitution makers would not be achieved. None

Interpretation of General Entry visavis Tax Entries Principles of interpretation on the conflicting entries cannot be placed in a strait jacket formula.	None
Rule of interpretation will vary, depending upon the subject matter of legislation.	None
A view that power of taxation may not be found in a general entry would be too simplistic to bear the test of constitutional interpretation.	None
Regulating statute may contain taxing provisions.	None
A statute, yet again, may contain both general provisions as also the taxing ones.	None
The decisions of the Privy Council in Gov.Gen. in Coucil Vs.	None
Madras 1945 FCR 179 on the question of interpretation as regard conflicting legislative entries in general and tax entries in particular may not be apposite in the instant case inasmuch herein we are concerned with only one question, namely, whether the field of taxation of mines and minerals which are extracted and ceases to be a part of the surface is wholly covered or not.	None
One of the principles for reconciling conflicting tax entries is to ascertain as to whether a person, thing or activity is the subject matter of tax and the amount of the tax to be levied.	None
The question which has to be answered on the basis of the aforementioned principle is, is it a tax on land or tax on mineral.	None
If having regard to the nature of tax and keeping in view the history of the legislation to the effect that the State of West Bengal has all along been trying to impose tax on minerals as opposed to tax on land, is taken into consideration, it will be noticed that endeavours have been made to continue to impose cess on mineral and mineral rights in the garb of land tax.	None
The decisions of this Court as referred to hereinbefore including India Cement (supra) must be judged from this angle and not in vacuum.	None
It may be true that taxation is regarded as a distinct matter and has separately set out in List I or List II of the Seventh Schedule of the Constitution of India but the what should be borne in mind is that the same by itself is not determinative of the nature of the statute.	None
There are statutes and statutes one statute may cover general entry as also a taxation entry whereas another may be enacted only in terms of the general entry and third in terms of a tax entry.	None
In M.P. Sundarramier Co.	None
Vs.	Decision
State of Andhra Pradesh Anr.	None
1958	None
SCR 1422, this Court was concerned with the validity of imposition of tax on interState sales under the Madras General Sales Tax Act and was not dealing with a matter of this nature.	None
It fact that under the constitutional scheme taxation is regarded as a distinct matter and is separately set out is not decisive for the purpose of determining the validity thereof.	None
There exist a large number of statutes and indeed the constitutional scheme permits imposition of tax to regulate a particular trade.	None
156.	None
the observation in Synthetics Chemicals Vs.	None
State of U.P. (1991) SCC 109	None
that the tax may not be levied under a general entry although may be correct but the same would not mean that a regulatory fee which is in the nature of a tax cannot also be imposed.	None
No hard and fast rule can, therefore, be laid down and each case has to be considered on its own merit.	None
Can it, therefore, be said that a regulating statute being a general statute, no tax thereunder could be imposed.	None
It may not be necessary for us to delve deep into the matter as to whether power of regulation and control is separate and distinct from the power of taxation.	None
Generally speaking, it may be true that power to regulate would not carry with it the power to impose tax but the same does not have an universal application.	None
The question which would arise for consideration is whether constitutional scheme expressly permits such a legislation but the question which should be posed is as whether the constitutional scheme prohibits enactment of such a statute.	None
Such prohibition does not exist and in that view of the matter, it is permissible for the Parliament to enact a statute both in terms of a general entry as also a taxing entry.	None
No decision has been brought to our notice to suggest that the same is impermissible in our constitutional scheme.	None
As regard Entry 97 of List I, this Court in Union of India Vs.	None
Shri Harbhajan Singh Dhillon (1971) 2 SCC 779 held The last sentence applied much more to the Constitution of a sovereign democratic republic.	None
it is true that there are some limitations in Part III of the Constitution on the Legislatures in India but they are of a different character.	None
They have nothing to do with legislative competence.	None
If This is the true scope of residuary powers of Parliament, then we are unable to see why we should not, when dealing with a Central Act, enquire where it is legislation in respect of any matter in List II for this is the only field regarding which there is a prohibition against parliament.	None
If a Central Act does not enter or invade these prohibited fields there is no point in trying to decide as to under which entry or entries of List I or List III a Central Act would rightly fit in.	None
(See also Satpal Co. (1979) 3	None
SCR 1031).	None

This Court must also not forget that there exists a difference in interpretation between an entry relating to fee and entry relating to tax.	None
Once it is held that the matters in the State List is to the extent of declaration stand substracted from the scope and ambit of Entry 23 of the State List, even no fee can be levied which will come in the way of Central Governments power of regulation of mines.	None
Assuming royalty, deed rent and surface rent would not come within the purview of definition of tax, this Court is merely required to consider as to whether such a power exists in the Parliament or not.	None
The validity of the Mines and Minerals (Regulations and Development) Act is not in question.	None
Section 25 of the 1957 Act in no uncertain terms states that any rent, royalty, tax, fee or other impost under the said Act or the rules made thereunder can be recovered as arrears of land revenue.	None
The very fact that the expression tax, fee or other sum due to the Government which could be imposed under the Act and the recovery thereof is the subject matter of Section 25 of the Act, this Court, as noticed hereinbefore, in a large number of decisions held that such a power to impose tax exists under the Act.	None
Question of recovery of tax would arise only when it is imposed under the Act or the rules framed thereunder.	None
Section 25 of the MMRD Act, 1957 by necessary implication refers to the taxing power of the Parliament.	None
Imposition of taxes on minerals rights would affect the development of mines and minerals.	None
the Parliaments authority to regulate and control mineral development would be seriously impaired and affected if it is held that the matter relating to imposition of tax on mineral is also vested in the State.	None
The vires of Section 9 and 9A of the 1957 Act has not been questioned.	None
In fact, they have been held to be intravires in State of M.P. Vs.	None
Mahalaxmi Fabric Mills Ltd. 1995 Supp.	None
(1) SCC 642.	None
Saurashtra Cement and Chemical Industries Ltd.	None
Vs.	Decision
Union of India (2001) 1 SCC 91 and South Eastern Coalfields Ltd. (supra).	None
Unless power to levy compulsory impost is held to be ultra vires the Constitution, it cannot be held that the Parliament has incroached upon the States power of taxation.	None
Furthermore, Entry 36 of List I of the Government of India Act, 1935 was the corresponding provision of Entry 54 of List I of the Constitution.	None
Similarly, Entries 23 and 44 were the corresponding provisions in the List II in the Government of India Act containing identical provisions as in Entries 23 and 50 of List II of the Constitution.	None
Mines and Minerals (Regulation and Development) Act, 1948 was enacted which was refereable to Entry 36 of List I of the 1935 Act.	None
the said 1948 Act inter alia contains provisions for levy of taxes.	None
See Section 6(2) of the 1948 Act.	None
The history of legislation as regard regulation of mine and development of mineral is a pointer to the fact that Section (6)(2) of the 1948 Act not only provided for prohibition of the mining, quarrying or digging or the excavating or collecting of minerals from any mine or in any area, but also provided for imposition of tax.	None
Nobody says that by reason of rule making power, a tax can be levied under Section 13(2)(i) but what has been held by this Court is that the field of imposition of tax, fee or any other sum has been conferred on the Parliament under the Mines and Mineral (Regulation and Development) Act itself by necessary implication or otherwise as otherwise there would not have been any reason for the Parliament to say that such tax, fee or any other sum due to the Government under this Act meaning thereby 1951 Act or the rules framed thereunder would be recoverable.	None
It may also be true that by reason of rule making power as contained in Section 13(2) and Section 15(1A) the Parliament has not delegated the power to impose tax upon the Central Government or the State Government, as the case may be.	None
This might have been done considering the fact that the Parliament would make use of it, as and when occasion arises therefor.	None
The Parliament by enacting Sections 25 both in the 1957 Act and the 1953 Act reserved the authority upto itself to imposed any other tax falling in List I. the Parliament may also impose a tax which otherwise would not fall in any one of the taxing entries but may fall under the residuary entry being Entry 97.	Arguments of Respondent
Only because in Section 1392) or Section 15 (1A) of the Act power to impose tax has not been delegated, the same would not mean that the field in relation thereto is not covered as the said expression specifically finds place in Section 25 of the Act.	None
The expressions under this Act or the rules made thereunder as significant.	None
In Hingir Rampur Coal Co. Ltd.	None
Vs.	Decision
The State of Orissa Ors.	Reasoning
(AIR 1961 SC 459 ) and State of Orissa Vs.	None
M.A. Tulloch 1964 (4) SCR 461 , the interpretation of Section 25 under Act No. 67 of 1957 Act did not fall for consideration.	None
Recovery of tax is an incident of imposition of tax.	None
Tax has three elements (i) taxing event (ii) assessment and (iii) recovery.	None
Recovery of a tax is part of the taxing statute.	None
The provision of another Parliamentary Act cannot be resorted to for realisation of tax imposed by the State or vice versa.	None
The power to impose tax, therefore, cannot be traced to Section 13 alone but must also be traced to Section 25.	None
If that view is taken, it would not be necessary to apply the principle of ejusdem generis for the purpose of interpretation of Section 2(1) and 13(2)(i) of the Act.	None
Those taxes fees and charges which would come in the way of regulation of mine and mineral development should be held to have been forbidden.	None
So read Sections 13(2) and 25 can be given an appropriate meaning.	None
It will, therefore, not be correct to say that Section 25 can be construed to be containing only a recovery provision.	None
The question, it will bear repetition to state, would be not that as to whether any tax, fee or any other charges of whatever nature have been levied under the 1957 Act but the question would be whether the field in respect thereof is covered or not.	None
In that view of the matter, the question of inference as regard the power to tax by necessary implication or otherwise would not arise.	None
For the aforementioned purpose what would not arise.	None
For the aforementioned purpose what would be required to be considered is to read Sections 13, 18 and 25 together harmoniously.	None
It may be true that in Section 25 the Parliament has not explicitly stated as to tax would be due to whom but that would not mean the provision is vague.	None
That would simply mean that whosoever would be entitled to the impost can take recourse thereto.	None
Under the 1957 Act, it is the State Governments who are the beneficiaries but that is of not much consequence.	None
M.A. Tulloch (supra) must be read in the aforementioned context and so read the logical corollary would be that the field for levy of tax, fee of other charges must be held to have been covered under the 1957 Act.	None
Entry 97, List I of the Seventh Schedule of the Constitution of India indisputably should be taken recourse to as a last resort but once it is held that the Parliament has expressed its intension to cover the field of taxation also under the 1957 Act, source of such power must be traced to the appropriate entries in List I including Entry 97, whence if no other source is traceable.	None
The matter may be considered from another angle.	None
The States on their own showing are entitled to levy tax upon exercising the power which are said to be in terms of Entries 49 and 50 of List II and in that view of the matter Section 25 of the Act can be taken recourse to for the purpose of recovery of tax imposed in terms of the statute enacted by the State.	None
To put it differently, the provisions of the Central Act which is said to be meant for recovery of the tax, fee and other charges imposed in terms of provisions thereof or the rules made thereunder cannot be resorted for recovery of any tax made by the State in terms of its taxing power under any of the entries contained in List II of the Seventh Schedule of the constitution of India.	None
Section 25 of the 1957 Act could have been taken recourse to for the purpose of recovery of dues to the State provided the State Act was inter linked with the Parliamentary Act or the same was otherwise permissible in terms of the constitutional scheme.	None
177.	None
it is now a well settled principle of law that words in a statute should be so construed so as not to be considered as surplages or superfluous.	None
Each would, as is wellknown must be given its proper meaning.	None
If the aforementioned principle of interpretation of statute is applied, it must be held that the Parliament made its intention clear so as to cover the entire field including the field of taxation as otherwise there is absolutely no reason as to why consciously the words tax, fee or any other charges have been used in Section 25 of the Act.	None
The decision in Union of India vs. Shri Harbhajan Singh Dhillon (1991) 2 SCC 779 is also relevant in the context.	None
In the said decision, this Court was concerned with the provision of Section 24 of the Finance Act, 1969 whereby the definition net wealth in the Wealth Tax Act was amended including the agricultural land in assets for the purpose of calculating tax on the capital value of the net wealth.	None
The High Court held the said provision as unconstitutional.	None
The majority speaking through Sikri, CJ, gave effect to Article 248 of the Constitution of India stating We must also mention that no material has been placed before us to show that it was ever in the mind of anybody, who had to deal with the making of the Constitution, that it was the intention to prohibit all the Legislature in this country from legislating on a particular topic.	None
In the said decision, therefore, it was held that the Parliament in certain situation has the legislative competence to impose tax touching agricultural activities although agriculture comes within the legislative domain of the State legislature.	None
Such a finding was arrived at having regard to the fact that the Parliament was aware that specific provision may not be found in the three Lists for the purpose of imposition of all types of taxes and in that situation Entry 97 of List I could be taken recourse to.	None
But the question as to why the Parliament did not confer any power to tax the capital value of land as an asset either on the Central Government or the State Government does not fall for our consideration in this case.	None
It an occasion arises, such a question has to be considered on its own merits, but the fact remains that so far as mines and minerals are concerned, levy of tax thereupon in any manner whatsoever is not within the power of the State.	None
The State cannot assume such power indirectly by seeking to impose tax on land which it cannot to directly.	None
So far as tea a concerned, power to impose excess duty on tea is expressly conferred on the Central Government in terms of Section 25 of the Tea Act.	None
The decision in Harbhajan Singh Dhillon (supra) was followed in Union of India and Anr.	None
Vs.	Decision
Delhi High Court Bar Assn.	None
Ors.	None
(2002) 4 SCC 275 .	None
The decision in His Holiness Kesavananda Bharati Sripadgalbvaru etc. vs. State of Kerala and Another (1973) 4 SCC 225 cannot be read mean that Entry 97 is honest in the eye of law.	None
It will bear repetition to state that it is not a case where we are concerned with the validity of the tax imposed by the Parliament but we are only concerned with the interpretation of a statute in terms of the constitutional scheme of distribution of legislative fields for the purpose of ascertaining as to whether the entire field is covered by the Parliament Act or not.	None
Once it is held that the entire field of mines and minerals as also on tea including the power to impose any tax as covered by the 1953 and 1957 , the impugned tax by way of levy of cess on coal and tea must be held to the ultra vires.	None
The question as to whether the power to impose tax must be express or not is of no moment inasmuch it does not arise for our consideration.	None
Levy of excise duty on minerals is permissible in terms of Entry 86 of List I, so is power to impose income tax on profits and gains from business of mining.	None
The question as to whether the power to tax must be express or not could have been gone into had the vires of taxing statute fallen for our consideration and not otherwise.	None
The doctrine of enforcement of police power is not applicable in India.	None
Power to regulate the trade and for the said purpose imposition of tax is wellknown in India.	None
Mines and Minerals (Regulation and Development) Act is also a regulatory statute.	None
In the State of Punjab and Anr.	None
vs. M s. Devans Modern Brewaries Ltd. Anr. 2003	None
(10) SCALE 202 , majority of three Judges of a Constitution Bench of this Court upheld the levy of import tax on liquor which apparently was made by the State in terms of Entry 51, List II of the Seventh Schedule of the Constitution of India as a valid piece of legislation as if the same was enacted in exercise of the States regulatory power under Entry.	None
In that case, taxing statute has been upheld having been imposed by way of regulatory measure stating The High Court of Punjab proceeded to decide the case on a total wrong assumption that the import fee levied is in the nature of duty which cannot be imposed under the Excise Act, 1984 when in fact, the import fee levied is the price for parting with the privilege given to the licensee to import beer into the State and, therefore, the same is within the competence of the State to impose import fee.	None
I am of the view that the licensee besides the payment of duty etc. is to comply with such conditions as the State Government may impose while formulating the excise policy for the concerned year.	None
The State, in my view, is competent and entitled to impose excise duty or countervailing duty.	None
Besides there is no bar on the State to charge any other fees on account of consideration for the privilege provided to the licensee to trade in liquor which privilege he did not otherwise have.	None
Therefore, the licensee is liable to comply with the other conditions imposed by the State Government from time to time.	None
As held in many cases referred to supra the levy in dispute under challenge is an import levy Imposition to tax by way of regulatory measures, therefore, is permissible while enacting a regulatory statute.	None
Regulatory licence fee also has been held to be tax.	None
The decision of a SevenJudge Bench of this Court in Synthetics and Chemicals Ltd. Others vs. State of U.P. Others (1990) 1 SCC 109 is also an authority for the proposition that such regulatory measures by imposing tax is permissible in law.	None
It is also for that purpose reference to Entry 97 of List I of the Seventh Schedule of the Constitution of India assumes relevance.	None
In these matters, this Court is not concerned with an imposition of tax as a result whereof the trade or commerce in the commodity in question is affected.	None
In this case, the court is concerned with interpretation of statutes whereby the power of taxation on fixation of price thereof is vested in the Central Government under the Parliamentary Act, viz.	None
the 1957 Act and the Tea Act, 1953 and in that view of the matter the contention that the State has a plenary power of taxation loses significance.	None
Brother Lahoti, J. has referred from Cooley on Constitutional Law and G.P. Singhs Principles of Statutory Interpretation so as to emphasise the necessity of strict interpretation of a taxing statute.	None
Once a strict construction of a taxing statute is applied it is possible to hold that the exercise of the States jurisdiction is really an act of fraud on the constitution inasmuch while imposing tax on land it seeks to levy tax on mines and minerals or tea in relation whereto it has even no regulatory power.	None
Furthermore, we have noticed hereinbefore that the cess imposed by the State of West Bengal is not reasonable as the same will have a great repercussion on the activities on coal bearing land.	None
It may not be proper for the Court to venture into an enquiry as to whether the impugned tax would hamper mineral development or not but once it is found that it thinkers with the subject, having regard to the constitutional scheme the State would be denuded of its power.	None
If despite the same, a State chooses to exercise such power, its action will be fraudulent and cannot be supported for any purpose whatsoever, even if thereby a reasonable tax or fee has been levied.	None
With utmost respect, I may observe that this Court may be setting a wrong precedent to ignore larger Bench decisions of this Court relying on or on the basis of the comments made by an author, however, eminent he may be, as judicial discipline mandates that we follow binding precedents.	None
An author is entitled to criticise a judgment but such criticism cannot be the basis for ignoring binding decisions of larger benches.	None
The principles of reading a judgment is wellknown.	None
What is binding in terms of Article 141 of the Constitution of India is the ratio of the judgment.	None
The ratio decidend of a judgment is the reason assigned in support of the conclusion.	None
It the reasons contained in a judgment do not appeal to a subsequent Bench, the matter may be referred to a large Bench but so long the same is not done, the ratio can neither be watered down nor brushed aside.	None
India Cement (supra), Orissa Cement (supra) and others judgments of Coordinate Benches are binding on us.	None
Correctness or otherwise of the said judgments has not been questioned.	None
It would, therefore, not be proper for this Court to read something in the judgment which does not appear therefrom or to exclude from our consideration reasonings on the basis whereof, the conclusions of the judgment had been reached.	None
If imposition of a regulatory fee is permissible on mineral of tea then the power therefor must be held to be in the Central Government having regard to the 1957 Act and the 1953 Act.	None
If the subject matter of tax is land, the power is with the State Government unless its power is denuded or otherwise limited.	None
However, anything which entrenches upon the field of Regulation of Mines and Minerals Development or industrial activities whether by reason of levy of any tax or impost, would necessarily be forbidden.	None
Entry 49 List II  Interpretation of General Entry 49 of List II confers legislative competence upon the State to impose tax on Land and Building.	None
Coalbearing land or mineral bearing land for the purpose of Entry 49, however, may not be equated with the land as ordinarily understood.	None
Land in its ordinary meaning may be an agricultural land or a nonagricultural land.	None
It may also be mineral bearing land.	None
Mineral bearing lands, however, are governed by the provisions of the 1957 Act and the rules framed thereunder, so far as the same is covered by the declaration contained in the statute.	None
In terms of the provisions of the said Act, cess, dead rent, as well as surface rent are payable.	None
Tea Industry is governed by 1953 Act.	None
The effect of the Union Legislation visavis the State Legislation on the same subject recently came up for consideration before a Bench of this Court.	None
Despite holding that the State has the power to levy market fee, this Court observed that seeds which would otherwise come within the purview of the definition of wheat would not be subject to such levy having regard to the provisions of Parliamentary Act known as the Seeds Act 1966.	None
(See Krishi Utpadan Mandi Samiti Ors.	None
vs. Pilibhit Pantnagar Beej Ltd. Anr. 2003	None
(10) SCALE 432 .	None
When, thus, the field is covered by Parliamentary Legislations, an effort has to be made that a conflict with a State Legislation is avoided.	None
Entry 49, List II visavis 1957 Act In assessing the field covered by an Act of Parliament, one has to be guided not merely by the actual provisions of the Act or the Rules made thereunder, but should also take into account matters and aspects which can be legitimately brought with the scope of the statute.	None
In this case, we are concerned with the interpretation of two entries in List I and List II of the Seventh Schedule of the Constitution of India.	None
The legislative competence in terms of Entry 49 List II is to be considered in the light of Entry 54 List I.	None
In a case of this nature, the court cannot raise a presumption of constitutionality of the State Act as the ultimate answer to the question will have to be ascertained as to which extent the field is covered.	None
If the tax on mines and minerals is subject matter which is covered under the 1957 Act, the power of the State must be held to be denuded.	None
Entry 49 of List II, however, should be read in such a manner so that the surface land must have a direct nexus with the subsoil right which is an inchoate right.	None
Indisputably, subsoil right would include mineral right.	None
Mining lease for winning of coal may be granted for huge area but depending on the nature of mining activities to be carried on, necessarily the mining lessee would not require the entire surface thereof except where mineral is being extracted by adopting quarrying method.	None
A mineral can be extracted from beneath a town.	None
village, national highway, railway track etc. in any manner, without disturbing the surface itself, subject of course upon carrying out the activities in such a scientific manner so that proper and adequate support to the surface is provided.	None
Mineral right may extend to more that one town or village.	None
Thus, there can be separate owners for the surface and the underground.	None
The right of the owner of the surface would necessarily cast a statutory or a contractual liability upon the mining lessee to provide the requisite support to the surface so as not to cause subsidence thereof.	None
If a wide definition of coal bearing land is given so as to hold that the State is entitled to levy tax on extracted mineral which is severed from land, the same would lead to an incongruous result as thereby value of part of the land itself would be a subject matter of measure of tax although they do not remain land as such.	None
In any event, coal severed from land cannot be said to be yield on coal bearing land so as to hold that the value thereof can be determined only for the purpose of measure of tax visavis the nature and character thereof.	None
A tax on land can be imposed so long a land exists.	None
Where, however, for the purpose of extraction of a mineral, the land was dug and the restoration was sought to be made by imposition and Improvement of Degraded Forest land Taxation Act, 1992, this Court in State of Bihar and Others.	None
Vs. Indian Aluminium Company and Others.	None
(1997) 8 SCC 360 distinguishing Goodricke Group Ltd. (supra) and following State of Orissa Vs. Mahanadi Coalfields Ltd. 1995 Supp.	Reasoning
2 SCC 686 , Orissa Cement (supra), India Cement (supra) and other cases observed 14 While upholding the validity of the Act this Court held that Entry 49 of List II of the Seventh Schedule contemplates the levy of tax on lands and buildings or both as units.	None
Tax on lands and building is directly imposed on lands and buildings and bears a definite relation to it 15Therefore, in order that a tax can be levied under Entry 49 of List II it is essential that land as a unit must exist on which the tax is imposed 16Therefore, in pith and substance it is a tax on activity on land and not on land itself.	None

it was further held Mr. Sibal placed strong reliance on the decision in the case of Goodricke Group Ltd. v. State of W.B. 1995 Supp.	None
(1) SCC 707 in support of his contention that the levy was on land itself and that the Act would be covered by Entry 49.	None
Goodricke case is clearly distinguishable.	None
There education cess and rural employment cess were levied on certain lands and buildings in the State of West Bengal.	None
The estates were carved out as a separate category and a different rate was prescribed therefor.	None
The cess on tea estates was calculated on the basis of yield of tea whereas cess on other lands was determined having regard to the development value of the same.	None
It was held that the tax was upon land though the cess was quantified on the basis of produce of the tea estate.	None
In the present case, however, we do not find that the tax is on land.	None
In fact what is sought to be taxed is in the absence of land.	None
It was opined One of the facets of tax being levied on land is that the primary responsibility of the payment of tad is on the owner of the land.	None
In the instant case the levy is not on the general ownership of the land but is on the person who uses it and who may or may not be the owner.	None
The primary liability is on the use by the occupier and if the occupier and the owner are two different persons the liability would be that of the occupier alone and not of the owner.	None
It was further held From the aforesaid discussion it is obvious that the present tax is one is obvious that the present that is one on the excavation and use of forest land and not on the forest land as such.	None
Taxing of the undertaking of a nonforest activity in a forest land cannot be regarded as being covered by Entry 49 of the State List because what is sought to be taxed is not land but the tax is on absence of land or forest by reason of the activity of excavation and or mining or use of forest land for a nonforest purpose.	None
The High Court was, therefore, right in allowing the writ petitions filed by the respondent.	None
Underlining is mine for emphasis It is, therefore, not correct to contend that While purporting to impose tax on land and buildings a State has the legislative competence in terms of Entry 49 of List II of the Constitution while in effect and substance it will entrench upon Entry 52 or Entry 54 of List I thereof.	None
